Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
The Three Possibilities for Two Beings
Ghazali enumerates three possibilities for two beings and presents the philosophers' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities for the relationship between two beings and why the philosophers argue that only one scenario leads to the concept of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
For two beings, only one can be necessary; the others imply dependence or impossibility.

Ghazali begins by laying out the rational options for how two beings could exist. If both are independent, that would be impossible duality. If both depend on each other, neither is necessary. Only one dependent on the other seems plausible, but the opponent argues that even this leads to a cause in the Necessary Being. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the division of possibilities
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the three scenarios: both independent, both dependent, or one dependent on the other.
Note the terms used: 'التثنية المطلقة' (absolute duality) and 'واجب الوجود' (necessary existence).
2
Understand the definition of Necessary Existence
The passage defines Necessary Existence as that which is self-subsisting and independent of all else. This definition is used to reject the first two possibilities.
Compare with the third possibility, where one depends on the other.
3
Recognize the opponent's conclusion
The opponent argues that the third possibility leads to the dependent being an effect, and the Necessary Being would be connected to a cause, which they find problematic.
This sets up the debate for the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتاج الآخر؛ فإن فرض كل واحد مستغنياً، فهما واجبا وجود، وهذه التثنية المطلقة؛ وهو محال.
وإما (١) أن يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر، فلا يكون واحد منهما واجب الوجود، إذ معنى واجب الوجود، ما قوامه بذاته، وهو مستغن من كل وجه عن غيره، فما احتاج إلى غيره، فذلك الغير علته، إذ لو رفع ذلك الغير، لامتنع وجوده، فلا يكون وجوده من ذاته، بل من غيره.
وإن قيل (٢): أحدهما يحتاج دون الآخر، فالذي يحتاج معلول، والواجب الوجود هو الآخر، ومهما كان معلولا افتقر إلى سبب، فيؤدى إلى أن ترتبط ذات واجب الوجود بسبب.
والاعتراض: على هذا أن يقال: المختار من هذه الأقسام هو القسم الأخير. ولكن إبطالكم القسم الأول وهو التثنية المطلقة، قد بينا أنه لا برهان لكم عليه، في المسألة التي قبل هذه، وأنها لا تتم إلا بالبناء على نفى الكثرة في هذه المسألة وما بعدها فها هو فرع هذه المسألة، كيف تنبنى هذه المسألة عليه؟!
ولكن المختار أن يقال: الذات فى قوامها غير محتاجة إلى الصفات، والصفات محتاجة إلى الموصوف كما في حقنا.
فيبقى قولهم: إن المحتاج إلى غيره لا يكون واجب الوجود.
فيقال لهم: إن أردتم بواجب الوجود أنه ليس له علة فاعلة، فلم قلتم ذلك (٣)؟! ولم استحال أن يقال: كما أن ذات واجب الوجود قديم، ولا فاعل له، فكذلك صفته قديمة معه، ولا فاعل لها؛ وإن أردتم بواجب الوجود (٤) أن لا تكون له علة قابلية فهو (٥) ليس بواجب الوجود على هذا التأويل، ولكنه مع هذا قديم، ولا فاعل له، فما المحيل لذلك؟!
فإن قيل: واجب الوجود المطلق هو الذى ليست له علة فاعلية ولا قابلية، فإذا سلم أن له علة قابلية، فقد سلم كونه معلولا.
قلنا: تسمية الذات القابلة علة قابلية، من اصطلاحكم؛ والدليل لم يدل على ثبوت واجب وجود، بحكم اصطلاحكم، وإنما دل على إثبات طرف ينقطع به تسلسل العلم والمعلولات، ولم يدل إلا على هذا القدر، وقطع التسلسل ممكن بواحد له صفات قديمة، لا فاعل لها، كما أنه لا فاعل لذاته، ولكنها تكون متقررة في ذاته، فليطرح لفظ واجب الوجود فإنه يمكن اللبس فيه، فإن البرهان لم يدل إلا على قطع التسلسل، ولم يدل على غيره البتة، فدعوى غيره تحكم.
فإن قيل: كما يجب قطع التسلسل فى العلة الفاعلية يجب قطعه في القابلية، إذ لو افتقر كل موجود إلى محل يقوم فيه، وافتقر المحل أيضاً إلى محل لزم التسلسل، كما لو افتقر كل موجود إلى علة، وافتقرت العلة أيضاً إلى علة.
قلنا: صدقتم، فلا قطعنا هذا التسلسل أيضاً، وقلنا: إن الصفة في ذاته، وليست ذاته قائمة بغيره، كما أن علمنا فى ذاتنا، وذاتنا محل له، وليس ذاتنا في محل، فالصفة انقطع تسلسل علتها (٦) الفاعلية مع الذات، إذ لا فاعل لها، كما لا فاعل للذات، بل لم تزل الذات بهذه الصفة، موجودة بلا علة لها لصفتها، وأما العلة القابلة فلم ينقطع تسلسلها إلا على الذات، ومن أين أن ينتفى المحل حين تنتفى العلة، والبرهان ليس يضطر إلا إلى قطع التسلسل، فكل طريق أمكن قطع التسلسل به، فهو وفاء بقضية البرهان الداعي إلى واجب الوجود.
And the other needs; for if each is assumed independent, then both are necessary of existence, and this is absolute duality; and it is impossible. Or (1) that each of them needs the other, so neither of them is necessary of existence, for the meaning of necessary of existence is that whose subsistence is by itself, and it is independent in every respect from other than it; so what needs another, then that other is its cause, for if that other were removed, its existence would be impossible, so its existence is not from itself but from another. And if it is said (2): One of them needs without the other, so the one that needs is an effect, and the necessary of existence is the other, and whenever it is an effect it is in need of a cause, leading to the essence of the Necessary of existence being connected to a cause. The objection: to this it is said: The chosen among these divisions is the last division. But your refutation of the first division, which is absolute duality, we have shown that you have no proof for it, in the preceding issue, and that it is only completed by building upon the negation of multiplicity in this issue and what follows; so this is a branch of that issue, how can this issue be built upon it?! But the chosen is to say: The essence in its subsistence is not in need of attributes, and attributes are in need of the described, as in our case. So their statement remains: The one in need of another cannot be necessary of existence. So it is said to them: If you mean by necessary of existence that which has no efficient cause, then why do you say that (3)?! And why is it impossible to say: Just as the essence of the Necessary of Existence is eternal and has no agent, so too His attribute is eternal with Him and has no agent; and if you mean by necessary of existence (4) that it has no receptive cause, then it (5) is not necessary of existence according to this interpretation, but it is eternal with this, and has no agent, so what makes that impossible?! For if it is said: The absolute necessary of existence is that which has neither efficient nor receptive cause; so if it is conceded that it has a receptive cause, then its being an effect is conceded. We say: Naming the receptive essence a receptive cause is from your terminology; and the proof did not indicate the affirmation of a necessary of existence according to your terminology; it only indicated the establishment of a terminus at which the regress of causes and effects is cut off, and it indicated only this extent; and cutting off the regress is possible by a being having eternal attributes, having no agent for them, just as there is no agent for its essence, but they are established in its essence. So let the term 'Necessary of Existence' be set aside, for confusion may arise in it; for the proof indicates only cutting off regress, and does not indicate anything else at all; so claiming other than that is arbitrary. For if it is said: Just as cutting off regress in the efficient cause is necessary, so too cutting off regress in the receptive cause is necessary; for if every existent were in need of a locus in which it subsists, and the locus also were in need of a locus, regress would follow, just as if every existent were in need of a cause and the cause also were in need of a cause. We say: You have spoken truth; so we cut off this regress as well, and we say: The attribute is in His essence, and His essence does not subsist by another, just as our knowledge is in our essence, and our essence is a locus for it, and our essence is not in a locus; so the attribute—the regress of its efficient cause (6) is cut off with the essence, for there is no agent for it, just as there is no agent for the essence; rather the essence has never ceased to be with this attribute, existing without a cause for its attribute. As for the receptive cause, its regress is not cut off except at the essence; and from where is it that the locus is negated when the cause is negated? The proof does not compel except to cut off regress; so every way by which cutting off regress is possible fulfills the requirement of the proof that leads to the Necessary of Existence.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the possibility of eternal attributes for the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). It begins with a rational division of possibilities for two beings: either both are independent (impossible, as that would entail absolute duality), or both depend on each other (impossible, as that would negate necessary existence), or one depends on the other (the chosen division). The opponents argue that if one depends on the other, it is an effect and causes a relation in the Necessary Being. Ghazali objects that the refutation of the first division (absolute duality) is not proven without assuming the negation of multiplicity, which is circular. He then states the Ash'ari position: the essence does not need attributes, but attributes need the essence, similar to human attributes. The opponents' remaining objection is that what needs another cannot be necessary of existence. Ghazali responds by questioning their definition: if 'necessary of existence' means having no efficient cause, then attributes can be co-eternal with no agent, just like the essence. If they mean having no receptive cause (i.e., no locus), then the attribute's need for the essence as a locus does not make the essence contingent; the essence remains eternal and without an agent. The proof for the Necessary Being only establishes the termination of regress; it does not specify that the terminus must be without attributes. Thus, a being with eternal attributes satisfies the proof. The final objection is that regress must be cut off in receptive causes as well, otherwise there would be infinite regress of loci. Ghazali agrees but responds that the attribute's receptive cause (the essence) stops the regress: the essence is not in another locus, just as human knowledge is in the self and the self is not in a locus. The efficient cause regress stops because the attributes have no agent, just as the essence has no agent. Therefore, the proof is satisfied, and the opponents' insistence on a purely simple being is arbitrary.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
التثنية المطلقة
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the third possibility (one dependent on the other) is logically unproblematic and does not lead to any issue.
Correction: The opponent argues that this possibility implies that the Necessary Being's essence is connected to a cause (the dependent), which they consider a violation of necessary existence. This sets up Ghazali's response.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's passage, what is the meaning of 'necessary of existence' (واجب الوجود)?
A) That which has no cause at all.
B) That whose subsistence is by itself and independent of all else.
C) That which is eternal.
D) That which has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The rational division of possibilities for two beings shows that only the scenario where one depends on the other avoids immediate contradiction, but it raises the issue of causal relation to the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If absolute duality is impossible, then only Allah is self-sufficient. Do you ever live as if something else—your career, relationships, or health—is ultimately independent and not completely dependent on Allah? How can you realign your heart and actions to acknowledge His sole aseity today?
?
The philosophers worried that Allah's essence being connected to a cause (His creation) is problematic. Do you harbor the belief that your actions or needs can 'cause' Allah to change or become obligated? How does this passage challenge you to trust in His complete self-sufficiency?
?
When you face a dilemma, do you unconsciously rely on a 'dual necessity'—trusting both Allah and your own plans equally? Commit to one concrete situation this week where you will consciously depend solely on Allah, affirming that He alone is the Necessary Being.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
10 min
Ghazali's Objection and the Ash'ari Position
Ghazali objects to the proof of absolute duality and presents the Ash'ari view of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the philosophers' refutation of 'absolute duality' is circular, and the Ash'ari position that attributes are dependent on the essence but not vice versa.

Core Idea
The proof against absolute duality is not independent; it relies on the very point at issue.

Ghazali now responds to the philosophers' argument. He points out that their refutation of the first possibility (absolute duality) is not proven independently; it depends on assuming the negation of multiplicity, which is the point under debate. He then states the Ash'ari position: the essence does not need attributes, but attributes need the essence, similar to human attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read how Ghazali claims that the philosophers' refutation of absolute duality is not proven without assuming the negation of multiplicity in later issues.
This is a methodological critique: the proof is circular.
2
Grasp the Ash'ari position
Ghazali states that the essence is independent of attributes, but attributes are dependent on the essence. This mirrors the human case where the self (essence) is a locus for knowledge.
This preserves the distinction between essence and attributes without making the essence dependent.
3
Note the remaining objection
The opponents still object that what needs another cannot be necessary. This sets up the next lesson's response.
The objection is based on the definition of necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتاج الآخر؛ فإن فرض كل واحد مستغنياً، فهما واجبا وجود، وهذه التثنية المطلقة؛ وهو محال.
وإما (١) أن يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر، فلا يكون واحد منهما واجب الوجود، إذ معنى واجب الوجود، ما قوامه بذاته، وهو مستغن من كل وجه عن غيره، فما احتاج إلى غيره، فذلك الغير علته، إذ لو رفع ذلك الغير، لامتنع وجوده، فلا يكون وجوده من ذاته، بل من غيره.
وإن قيل (٢): أحدهما يحتاج دون الآخر، فالذي يحتاج معلول، والواجب الوجود هو الآخر، ومهما كان معلولا افتقر إلى سبب، فيؤدى إلى أن ترتبط ذات واجب الوجود بسبب.

فإن قيل: واجب الوجود المطلق هو الذى ليست له علة فاعلية ولا قابلية، فإذا سلم أن له علة قابلية، فقد سلم كونه معلولا.
قلنا: تسمية الذات القابلة علة قابلية، من اصطلاحكم؛ والدليل لم يدل على ثبوت واجب وجود، بحكم اصطلاحكم، وإنما دل على إثبات طرف ينقطع به تسلسل العلم والمعلولات، ولم يدل إلا على هذا القدر، وقطع التسلسل ممكن بواحد له صفات قديمة، لا فاعل لها، كما أنه لا فاعل لذاته، ولكنها تكون متقررة في ذاته، فليطرح لفظ واجب الوجود فإنه يمكن اللبس فيه، فإن البرهان لم يدل إلا على قطع التسلسل، ولم يدل على غيره البتة، فدعوى غيره تحكم.
فإن قيل: كما يجب قطع التسلسل فى العلة الفاعلية يجب قطعه في القابلية، إذ لو افتقر كل موجود إلى محل يقوم فيه، وافتقر المحل أيضاً إلى محل لزم التسلسل، كما لو افتقر كل موجود إلى علة، وافتقرت العلة أيضاً إلى علة.
قلنا: صدقتم، فلا قطعنا هذا التسلسل أيضاً، وقلنا: إن الصفة في ذاته، وليست ذاته قائمة بغيره، كما أن علمنا فى ذاتنا، وذاتنا محل له، وليس ذاتنا في محل، فالصفة انقطع تسلسل علتها (٦) الفاعلية مع الذات، إذ لا فاعل لها، كما لا فاعل للذات، بل لم تزل الذات بهذه الصفة، موجودة بلا علة لها لصفتها، وأما العلة القابلة فلم ينقطع تسلسلها إلا على الذات، ومن أين أن ينتفى المحل حين تنتفى العلة، والبرهان ليس يضطر إلا إلى قطع التسلسل، فكل طريق أمكن قطع التسلسل به، فهو وفاء بقضية البرهان الداعي إلى واجب الوجود.

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the possibility of eternal attributes for the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). It begins with a rational division of possibilities for two beings: either both are independent (impossible, as that would entail absolute duality), or both depend on each other (impossible, as that would negate necessary existence), or one depends on the other (the chosen division). The opponents argue that if one depends on the other, it is an effect and causes a relation in the Necessary Being. Ghazali objects that the refutation of the first division (absolute duality) is not proven without assuming the negation of multiplicity, which is circular. He then states the Ash'ari position: the essence does not need attributes, but attributes need the essence, similar to human attributes. The opponents' remaining objection is that what needs another cannot be necessary of existence. Ghazali responds by questioning their definition: if 'necessary of existence' means having no efficient cause, then attributes can be co-eternal with no agent, just like the essence. If they mean having no receptive cause (i.e., no locus), then the attribute's need for the essence as a locus does not make the essence contingent; the essence remains eternal and without an agent. The proof for the Necessary Being only establishes the termination of regress; it does not specify that the terminus must be without attributes. Thus, a being with eternal attributes satisfies the proof. The final objection is that regress must be cut off in receptive causes as well, otherwise there would be infinite regress of loci. Ghazali agrees but responds that the attribute's receptive cause (the essence) stops the regress: the essence is not in another locus, just as human knowledge is in the self and the self is not in a locus. The efficient cause regress stops because the attributes have no agent, just as the essence has no agent. Therefore, the proof is satisfied, and the opponents' insistence on a purely simple being is arbitrary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the philosophers' proof against absolute duality is self-evident and independent.
Correction: Ghazali shows that it relies on assuming the negation of multiplicity, which is exactly what is being debated.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali object to the philosophers' refutation of absolute duality?
A) Because it is based on an incorrect definition of necessary existence.
B) Because it is circular, relying on the negation of multiplicity.
C) Because it assumes attributes are eternal.
D) Because it does not consider the third possibility.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali exposes the circularity in the philosophers' argument and offers the Ash'ari view that attributes are dependent on the essence, but the essence remains independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's essence is utterly independent of His attributes, yet your own attributes (like intelligence, strength) are entirely dependent on Him, how does that reorder your sense of self-sufficiency? In what area of your life are you acting as if you are independent of God?
?
Ghazali exposes a circular argument in the philosophers' reasoning. Can you identify a belief or assumption you hold about God that you have never critically examined? What would it mean to have that belief shown to be circular or unproven?
?
The Ash'ari position affirms that God's attributes are real and dependent on His essence. Do you truly believe that God's mercy and justice are real, personal qualities that affect you daily? If so, how should that change your actions today — especially when you face difficulty or feel distant from Him?

Divine Attributes
advanced
12 min
The Proof Only Requires Cutting Off Regress
Ghazali explains the proof's scope and defends attributes as compatible with the regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the proof for the Necessary Being only establishes a terminus for the regress of causes, not absolute simplicity, and that eternal attributes are compatible with this proof.

Core Idea
The proof cuts off regress; it does not require a being without attributes.

In this final part, Ghazali directly responds to the opponents' core objection. He distinguishes between efficient and receptive causes, and argues that the proof for the Necessary Being only shows the need to stop the regress of causes and effects. A being with eternal attributes, which have no agent and whose receptive cause is the essence, fully satisfies this proof. Let's read his detailed response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction of causes
Ghazali distinguishes between efficient cause (علة فاعلية) and receptive cause (علة قابلية). The opponents use both to argue against attributes.
Note that the attribute's efficient cause is nothing (no agent), and its receptive cause is the essence.
2
Follow the response on efficient cause
If 'necessary of existence' means having no efficient cause, then attributes can be co-eternal with no agent, just like the essence.
This defuses the objection that attributes need an efficient cause.
3
Follow the response on receptive cause
If 'necessary' means having no receptive cause, then attributes' need for the essence is not a problem; the essence remains uncaused and eternal.
The attribute's receptive cause is the essence itself, which stops the regress.
4
Grasp the final point on regress
The proof only requires cutting off the regress. Any way that achieves this—including a being with attributes—satisfies the proof.
Ghazali emphasizes that the term 'Wajib al-Wujud' can be misleading; the proof only proves a terminus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحتاج الآخر؛ فإن فرض كل واحد مستغنياً، فهما واجبا وجود، وهذه التثنية المطلقة؛ وهو محال.
وإما (١) أن يحتاج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر، فلا يكون واحد منهما واجب الوجود، إذ معنى واجب الوجود، ما قوامه بذاته، وهو مستغن من كل وجه عن غيره، فما احتاج إلى غيره، فذلك الغير علته، إذ لو رفع ذلك الغير، لامتنع وجوده، فلا يكون وجوده من ذاته، بل من غيره.
وإن قيل (٢): أحدهما يحتاج دون الآخر، فالذي يحتاج معلول، والواجب الوجود هو الآخر، ومهما كان معلولا افتقر إلى سبب، فيؤدى إلى أن ترتبط ذات واجب الوجود بسبب.
والاعتراض: على هذا أن يقال: المختار من هذه الأقسام هو القسم الأخير. ولكن إبطالكم القسم الأول وهو التثنية المطلقة، قد بينا أنه لا برهان لكم عليه، في المسألة التي قبل هذه، وأنها لا تتم إلا بالبناء على نفى الكثرة في هذه المسألة وما بعدها فها هو فرع هذه المسألة، كيف تنبنى هذه المسألة عليه؟!
ولكن المختار أن يقال: الذات فى قوامها غير محتاجة إلى الصفات، والصفات محتاجة إلى الموصوف كما في حقنا.
فيبقى قولهم: إن المحتاج إلى غيره لا يكون واجب الوجود.
فيقال لهم: إن أردتم بواجب الوجود أنه ليس له علة فاعلة، فلم قلتم ذلك (٣)؟! ولم استحال أن يقال: كما أن ذات واجب الوجود قديم، ولا فاعل له، فكذلك صفته قديمة معه، ولا فاعل لها؛ وإن أردتم بواجب الوجود (٤) أن لا تكون له علة قابلية فهو (٥) ليس بواجب الوجود على هذا التأويل، ولكنه مع هذا قديم، ولا فاعل له، فما المحيل لذلك؟!
فإن قيل: واجب الوجود المطلق هو الذى ليست له علة فاعلية ولا قابلية، فإذا سلم أن له علة قابلية، فقد سلم كونه معلولا.
قلنا: تسمية الذات القابلة علة قابلية، من اصطلاحكم؛ والدليل لم يدل على ثبوت واجب وجود، بحكم اصطلاحكم، وإنما دل على إثبات طرف ينقطع به تسلسل العلم والمعلولات، ولم يدل إلا على هذا القدر، وقطع التسلسل ممكن بواحد له صفات قديمة، لا فاعل لها، كما أنه لا فاعل لذاته، ولكنها تكون متقررة في ذاته، فليطرح لفظ واجب الوجود فإنه يمكن اللبس فيه، فإن البرهان لم يدل إلا على قطع التسلسل، ولم يدل على غيره البتة، فدعوى غيره تحكم.
فإن قيل: كما يجب قطع التسلسل فى العلة الفاعلية يجب قطعه في القابلية، إذ لو افتقر كل موجود إلى محل يقوم فيه، وافتقر المحل أيضاً إلى محل لزم التسلسل، كما لو افتقر كل موجود إلى علة، وافتقرت العلة أيضاً إلى علة.
قلنا: صدقتم، فلا قطعنا هذا التسلسل أيضاً، وقلنا: إن الصفة في ذاته، وليست ذاته قائمة بغيره، كما أن علمنا فى ذاتنا، وذاتنا محل له، وليس ذاتنا في محل، فالصفة انقطع تسلسل علتها (٦) الفاعلية مع الذات، إذ لا فاعل لها، كما لا فاعل للذات، بل لم تزل الذات بهذه الصفة، موجودة بلا علة لها لصفتها، وأما العلة القابلة فلم ينقطع تسلسلها إلا على الذات، ومن أين أن ينتفى المحل حين تنتفى العلة، والبرهان ليس يضطر إلا إلى قطع التسلسل، فكل طريق أمكن قطع التسلسل به، فهو وفاء بقضية البرهان الداعي إلى واجب الوجود.
And the other needs; for if each is assumed independent, then both are necessary of existence, and this is absolute duality; and it is impossible. Or (1) that each of them needs the other, so neither of them is necessary of existence, for the meaning of necessary of existence is that whose subsistence is by itself, and it is independent in every respect from other than it; so what needs another, then that other is its cause, for if that other were removed, its existence would be impossible, so its existence is not from itself but from another. And if it is said (2): One of them needs without the other, so the one that needs is an effect, and the necessary of existence is the other, and whenever it is an effect it is in need of a cause, leading to the essence of the Necessary of existence being connected to a cause. The objection: to this it is said: The chosen among these divisions is the last division. But your refutation of the first division, which is absolute duality, we have shown that you have no proof for it, in the preceding issue, and that it is only completed by building upon the negation of multiplicity in this issue and what follows; so this is a branch of that issue, how can this issue be built upon it?! But the chosen is to say: The essence in its subsistence is not in need of attributes, and attributes are in need of the described, as in our case. So their statement remains: The one in need of another cannot be necessary of existence. So it is said to them: If you mean by necessary of existence that which has no efficient cause, then why do you say that (3)?! And why is it impossible to say: Just as the essence of the Necessary of Existence is eternal and has no agent, so too His attribute is eternal with Him and has no agent; and if you mean by necessary of existence (4) that it has no receptive cause, then it (5) is not necessary of existence according to this interpretation, but it is eternal with this, and has no agent, so what makes that impossible?! For if it is said: The absolute necessary of existence is that which has neither efficient nor receptive cause; so if it is conceded that it has a receptive cause, then its being an effect is conceded. We say: Naming the receptive essence a receptive cause is from your terminology; and the proof did not indicate the affirmation of a necessary of existence according to your terminology; it only indicated the establishment of a terminus at which the regress of causes and effects is cut off, and it indicated only this extent; and cutting off the regress is possible by a being having eternal attributes, having no agent for them, just as there is no agent for its essence, but they are established in its essence. So let the term 'Necessary of Existence' be set aside, for confusion may arise in it; for the proof indicates only cutting off regress, and does not indicate anything else at all; so claiming other than that is arbitrary. For if it is said: Just as cutting off regress in the efficient cause is necessary, so too cutting off regress in the receptive cause is necessary; for if every existent were in need of a locus in which it subsists, and the locus also were in need of a locus, regress would follow, just as if every existent were in need of a cause and the cause also were in need of a cause. We say: You have spoken truth; so we cut off this regress as well, and we say: The attribute is in His essence, and His essence does not subsist by another, just as our knowledge is in our essence, and our essence is a locus for it, and our essence is not in a locus; so the attribute—the regress of its efficient cause (6) is cut off with the essence, for there is no agent for it, just as there is no agent for the essence; rather the essence has never ceased to be with this attribute, existing without a cause for its attribute. As for the receptive cause, its regress is not cut off except at the essence; and from where is it that the locus is negated when the cause is negated? The proof does not compel except to cut off regress; so every way by which cutting off regress is possible fulfills the requirement of the proof that leads to the Necessary of Existence.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the possibility of eternal attributes for the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). It begins with a rational division of possibilities for two beings: either both are independent (impossible, as that would entail absolute duality), or both depend on each other (impossible, as that would negate necessary existence), or one depends on the other (the chosen division). The opponents argue that if one depends on the other, it is an effect and causes a relation in the Necessary Being. Ghazali objects that the refutation of the first division (absolute duality) is not proven without assuming the negation of multiplicity, which is circular. He then states the Ash'ari position: the essence does not need attributes, but attributes need the essence, similar to human attributes. The opponents' remaining objection is that what needs another cannot be necessary of existence. Ghazali responds by questioning their definition: if 'necessary of existence' means having no efficient cause, then attributes can be co-eternal with no agent, just like the essence. If they mean having no receptive cause (i.e., no locus), then the attribute's need for the essence as a locus does not make the essence contingent; the essence remains eternal and without an agent. The proof for the Necessary Being only establishes the termination of regress; it does not specify that the terminus must be without attributes. Thus, a being with eternal attributes satisfies the proof. The final objection is that regress must be cut off in receptive causes as well, otherwise there would be infinite regress of loci. Ghazali agrees but responds that the attribute's receptive cause (the essence) stops the regress: the essence is not in another locus, just as human knowledge is in the self and the self is not in a locus. The efficient cause regress stops because the attributes have no agent, just as the essence has no agent. Therefore, the proof is satisfied, and the opponents' insistence on a purely simple being is arbitrary.

Key Terms
علة فاعلية
علة قابلية
تسلسل
صفات قديمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the proof for the Necessary Being requires a being with no attributes (absolute simplicity).
Correction: Ghazali argues that the proof only requires stopping the regress; a being with attributes that are eternal and without agent satisfies it.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the proof for the Necessary Being actually establish?
A) A being that has neither efficient nor receptive causes.
B) A being that must be absolutely simple without attributes.
C) A terminus at which the regress of causes and effects is cut off.
D) A being that must have an efficient cause.

Key Takeaway
The proof for the Necessary Being only requires a terminus for the regress; a being with eternal attributes that has no agent and whose essence is the locus of attributes fully meets this requirement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you unconsciously impose philosophical conditions (like absolute simplicity) that the proof itself does not require? What would it mean to accept God as He has described Himself, even if that includes eternal attributes? Are you willing to set aside your intellectual preferences and submit to what the proof actually shows?
?
Ghazali warns that the term 'Necessary of Existence' can cause confusion. Have you ever clung to a theological term without understanding its precise meaning, and in doing so, missed the deeper truth it points to? How might your faith be tested if you had to let go of a comfortable but imprecise concept of God?
?
The proof cuts off both efficient and receptive regress. But in your own life, do you seek to cut off infinite excuses and place the ultimate responsibility for your existence and attributes solely on God? Or do you subtly attribute your own existence to secondary causes, forgetting that He is the terminus of all regress? How can you live today in conscious awareness that you and all your attributes are ultimately from Him?

Refuting the Second Argument: Accidental Attributes
Ghazali refutes the claim that divine attributes being accidental implies they are caused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that calling divine attributes 'accidental' does not mean they are caused, and how he distinguishes between an efficient cause and a substrate.

Core Idea
Attributes subsist in the divine essence without being efficiently caused.

This passage from Ghazali's Tahafut addresses a philosophical argument against divine attributes. The philosophers claim that since human attributes are accidental to the essence, God's attributes must also be accidental and thus caused. Ghazali responds by clarifying what 'accidental' means in this context, showing that it does not imply an efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' argument
Read the passage to identify how the philosophers reason from human attributes to divine attributes, concluding that God would be caused.
Notice the parallel they draw between human and divine attributes.
2
Grasp Ghazali's distinction
Ghazali distinguishes two meanings of 'dependent on the essence': efficient cause vs. mere substrate. See how he rejects the first and admits the second.
3
Analyze the terminology critique
Ghazali argues that the philosophers use ugly terms like 'accidental' and 'caused' to make the idea seem absurd, but the real meaning is harmless.
This is a key rhetorical move.
4
Address the need objection
The philosophers object that attributes imply need. Ghazali refutes this by defining absolute independence as not needing what is other, but attributes are not 'other' in a problematic sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثانى قولهم : إن العلم والقدرة فينا ، ليسا داخلين في ماهية ذاتنا ، بل هما عارضان ، وإذا ثبتت هذه الصفات للأول ، لم تكن أيضاً داخلة في ماهية ذاته ، بل كانت عارضة بالإضافة إليه ، وإن كان دائماً له ؛ ورب عارض لا يفارق ، أو يكون لازماً للماهية ، ولا يصير بذلك مقوماً لذاتها وإذا كان عارضاً ، كان تابعاً للذات ، وكانت الذات سبباً فيه ، فكان معلولا ، فكيف يكون واجب الوجود . وهذا هو الأول مع تغيير عبارته . فنقول : إن عنيتم بكونه تابعاً للذات ، وكون الذات سبباً له ؛ أن الذات علة فاعلية له ، وأنه مفعول للذات ؛ فليس كذلك ، فإن ذلك ليس يلزم في علمنا بالإضافة إلى ذاتنا ، إذ ذاتنا ليست بعلة فاعلة لعلمنا وإن عنيتم أن الذات محل ، وأن الصفة لا تقوم بنفسها في غير محل ، فهذا مسلم ، فلم يمتنع هذا ؟! فإن عبر عنه بالتابع ، أو العارض ، أو المعلول ، أو ما أراده المعبر لم يتغير المعنى ، إذا لم يكن المعنى سوى أنه قائم بالذات قيام الصفات بالموصوفات ; ولم يستحيل أن يكون قائماً بالذات ، وهو مع ولا فاعل له ؟ ! فكل أدلتهم تهويل بتقبيح العبارة ، بتسميته ممكناً ، وجائزاً، وتابعاً ، ولازماً و معلولاً ، وأن ذلك مستنكراً ; فيقال : إن أريد بذلك أن له فاعلاً ، فليس كذلك ، وإن لم يرد به إلا أنه لا فاعل له ، ولكن له محل هو قائم فيه ، فليعبر عن هذا المعنى بأى عبارة أريد ، فلا استحالة فيه وربما هو لوا بتقبيح العبارة من وجه آخر ، فقالوا : هذا يؤدى إلى أن يكون الأول محتاجاً إلى هذه الصفات ، فلا يكون غنياً مطلقاً إذا الغنى المطلق من لا يحتاج إلى غير ذاته
The second path is their statement: Knowledge and power in us are not included in the quiddity of our essence, but rather they are accidental. And if these attributes are established for the First, they would also not be included in the quiddity of His essence, but would be accidental in relation to Him, even if they are eternal for Him. And perhaps an accident does not separate, or is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, and it does not thereby become constitutive of its essence. And if it is accidental, it is dependent on the essence, and the essence is a cause of it, so it is caused. So how could He be necessary of existence? This is the same as the first (argument) with a change of wording. So we say: If you mean by its being dependent on the essence, and the essence being a cause of it, that the essence is an efficient cause of it and it is an effect of the essence, then it is not so; for that is not necessary in our knowledge in relation to our essence, since our essence is not an efficient cause of our knowledge. And if you mean that the essence is a substrate and the attribute does not subsist by itself without a substrate, then this is admitted; so what makes this impossible? If one expresses it as 'dependent', 'accidental', 'caused', or whatever expression he wishes, the meaning does not change, as long as the meaning is only that it subsists in the essence in the manner of attributes subsisting in their subjects. And it is not impossible that it subsists in the essence, while it (the attribute) has no agent. All their arguments are intimidation by using ugly terminology: calling it possible, contingent, dependent, necessary, caused, and that this is objectionable. So it is said: If by that it is meant that it has an agent, then it is not so; and if nothing is meant by it except that it has no agent but has a substrate in which it subsists, then let this meaning be expressed by whatever expression one wishes; there is no impossibility in it. And perhaps it is more fitting to use ugly terminology from another aspect, for they say: This leads to the First being in need of these attributes, so He would not be absolutely independent (غني مطلق), for absolute independence is one who is not in need of anything other than His essence.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second philosophical argument against divine attributes. The philosophers argue that in us, knowledge and power are accidental (عارضان) to our essence, not part of our quiddity. If the First has these attributes, they would also be accidental to His essence, and thus dependent on it, making Him caused (معلول), contradicting necessary existence. Ghazali responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'dependent' or 'caused'. If it means the essence is an efficient cause (علة فاعلية) producing the attribute, then this does not follow even in us: our essence does not efficiently cause our knowledge. If it only means the essence is a substrate (محل) for the attribute, then that is admitted. Calling it 'dependent', 'accidental', or 'caused' does not change the fact that it merely means subsistence in a substrate. There is no impossibility in an attribute subsisting in an essence without having an efficient cause — the philosophers merely use ugly terminology to make it seem absurd. The objection that this implies need (حاجة) is also rejected: absolute independence (غنى مطلق) is defined as not needing anything other than His essence, but the attributes are not 'other' in the sense of being separate entities that He needs; they are subsistent in His essence. Ghazali thus upholds the possibility of eternal attributes without a cause, consistent with His being the Necessary of Existence.

Key Terms
عارض
ماهية
تابع للذات
علة فاعلية
محل
غنى مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling an attribute 'accidental' or 'dependent' automatically implies it has an efficient cause and is contingent.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'dependent' can simply mean subsisting in a substrate without an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the only correct interpretation of the attributes being 'dependent on the essence' (تابعة للذات)?
A) The essence efficiently produces the attributes
B) The attributes are contingent and created
C) The attributes subsist in the essence as a substrate
D) The attributes are separate entities that the essence needs

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are not efficiently caused; they subsist in the essence without implying need or contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes like His knowledge or power, do you ever instinctively think they are 'added' to His essence, as if He would be incomplete without them? How does Ghazali's distinction between efficient cause and substrate challenge this subtle assumption? What would it mean for your worship if you truly internalized that God's attributes are not 'things' He possesses but are identical with His essence?
?
The philosophers were troubled by the idea that divine attributes might make God dependent. But deep down, is there a part of you that feels uneasy attributing multiple attributes to God, fearing it might compromise His oneness? How does this passage refute that unease? Will you today consciously affirm that God's attributes are not separate entities that 'depend' on Him like effects, but are His very reality?
?
If you were to explain to a fellow believer that 'God is knowing, powerful, and living' without implying any need or cause, how would you use the concepts from this lesson? What would you change in your own internal conversation about God's nature after studying this refutation?

Refuting the Claim that Attributes are Effects
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' second argument on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that God's attributes are effects, and how he distinguishes between an attribute subsisting in the essence and being produced by an efficient cause.

Core Idea
Attributes can be eternally inherent in God without being caused effects.

Ghazali continues his critique by addressing the philosophers' second argument: that divine attributes like knowledge and power, being accidental in humans, must be accidental and thus caused in God. He carefully dismantles this by clarifying what 'dependent' really means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
Notice the two main sections separated by a line break.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Locate where the philosophers claim attributes are accidental and thus effects. Note their reasoning from human attributes.
The argument is in the second section starting with 'المسلك الثانى'.
3
Ghazali's distinction of 'dependent'
Ghazali distinguishes two meanings: efficient cause vs. locus. Find where he presents each and how he responds.
Pay attention to the phrases 'علة فاعلية' and 'محل'.
4
Understand the concession and rejection
He rejects the efficient cause meaning but concedes the locus meaning. See why he says this does not imply impossibility.
He uses the example of our own knowledge to argue against efficient causality.
5
Recognize the rhetorical strategy
Ghazali claims the philosophers use intimidating terminology. Understand his final point about wording not changing reality.
He lists terms like 'possible', 'contingent', 'dependent' as mere rhetoric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أريد بواجب الوجود شيء سوى موجود ليست له علة فاعلية حتى ينقطع به التسلسل ، فلا نسلم أن ذلك واجب أصلا ؛ ومهما اتسع العقل لقبول موجود قديم لا علة لوجوده اتسع لقبول قديم ،موصوف ، لا علة لوجوده في ذاته وفى صفاته جميعاً .
*
المسلك الثانى قولهم : إن العلم والقدرة فينا ، ليسا داخلين في ماهية ذاتنا ، بل هما عارضان ، وإذا ثبتت هذه الصفات للأول ، لم تكن أيضاً داخلة في ماهية ذاته ، بل كانت عارضة بالإضافة إليه ، وإن كان دائماً له ؛ ورب عارض لا يفارق ، أو يكون لازماً للماهية ، ولا يصير بذلك مقوماً لذاتها
وإذا كان عارضاً ، كان تابعاً للذات ، وكانت الذات سبباً فيه ، فكان معلولاً
فكيف يكون واجب الوجود . وهذا هو الأول مع تغيير عبارته . فنقول : إن عنيتم بكونه تابعاً للذات ، وكون الذات سبباً له ؛ أن الذات علة فاعلية له ، وأنه مفعول للذات ؛ فليس كذلك ، فإن ذلك ليس يلزم في علمنا بالإضافة إلى ذاتنا ، إذ ذاتنا ليست بعلة فاعلة لعلمنا وإن عنيتم أن الذات محل ، وأن الصفة لا تقوم بنفسها في غير محل ، فهذا مسلم ، فلم يمتنع هذا ؟! فإن عبر عنه بالتابع ، أو العارض ، أو المعلول ، أو ما أراده المعبر ؛ لم يتغير المعنى ، إذا لم يكن المعنى سوى أنه قائم بالذات قيام الصفات بالموصوفات ؛ ولم يستحيل أن يكون قائماً بالذات ، وهو مع ولا فاعل له ؟ ! فكل أدلتهم تهويل بتقبيح العبارة ، بتسميته ممكناً ، وجائزاً، وتابعاً ، ولازماً و معلولاً ، وأن ذلك مستنكراً ؛ فيقال : إن أريد بذلك أن له فاعلاً ، فليس كذلك ، وإن لم يرد به إلا أنه لا فاعل له ، ولكن له محل هو قائم فيه ، فليعبر عن هذا المعنى بأى عبارة أريد ، فلا استحالة فيه
And if by 'Necessary Existence' is meant something other than an existent that has no efficient cause such that regress is terminated by it, then we do not concede that that is necessary at all; and whenever the mind is capacious enough to accept an eternal existent with no cause for its existence, it is capacious enough to accept an eternal, qualified [existent], with no cause for its existence in its essence and all its attributes.
*
The second approach: their statement: 'Knowledge and power in us are not part of the quiddity of our essence, but rather they are accidents. And when these attributes are affirmed for the First, they also are not part of the quiddity of His essence, but are accidental in relation to Him, even if they are eternal for Him. And there may be an accident that does not separate [from the essence], or it may be a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, without thereby becoming constitutive of the essence. And if it is accidental, then it is dependent on the essence, and the essence is a cause of it, so it is an effect.' So how can it be Necessary Existence? This is the first [argument] with a change of wording. So we say: If by its being dependent on the essence, and the essence being a cause of it, you mean that the essence is an efficient cause of it, and that it is an effect of the essence, then it is not so; for this is not necessary in our knowledge in relation to our essence, since our essence is not an efficient cause of our knowledge. And if you mean that the essence is a locus [for it], and that the attribute does not subsist by itself without a locus, then this is conceded; so why is this impossible?! For if it is expressed as 'dependent', 'accident', 'effect', or whatever expression is used, the meaning does not change, as long as the meaning is nothing other than that it subsists through the essence in the manner of attributes subsisting through their subjects. And it is not impossible for it to subsist through the essence, while it is eternal and has no agent. All their arguments are mere intimidation by making the wording ugly, by calling it 'possible', 'contingent', 'dependent', 'necessary concomitant', 'effect', and that this is objectionable. So it is said: If by that is meant that it has an agent, then it is not so; and if nothing is meant by it except that it has no agent, but it has a locus in which it subsists, then let this meaning be expressed by whatever phrase is desired, for there is no impossibility in it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' second argument that God's attributes (like knowledge and power) are accidental and thus caused, which would undermine His necessity. Ghazali begins by stating the premise: if an eternal existent without cause can be accepted, then an eternal qualified existent without cause for its essence and attributes is equally acceptable. He then presents the philosophers' argument: in us, knowledge and power are accidental, so in God they would also be accidental, and being accidental makes them dependent on the essence and thus effects. Ghazali responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'dependent' or 'caused'. If it means the essence is an efficient cause producing the attribute, he denies this because in ourselves our essence is not an efficient cause of our knowledge. If it means the essence is a locus and the attribute subsists in it without being an efficient effect, then this is conceded. The philosophers' use of terms like 'possible', 'accident', 'dependent' is merely rhetorical intimidation. The core issue is whether an attribute can be eternally inherent in the essence without requiring an external cause. Ghazali affirms this is possible, and the philosophers' objection is based on a misuse of terminology.

علة فاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes being inherent in the essence necessarily implies they are effects requiring an efficient cause.
Correction: Inherence in a locus does not entail efficient causality; the attribute can subsist without being produced by the essence as an agent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali in this passage, what is the mistake in the philosophers' argument that attributes are effects?
A) They assume that being a necessary concomitant makes the attribute an effect.
B) They confuse the meaning of 'dependent' – it only means the attribute subsists in the essence, not that it is produced by an efficient cause.
C) They deny that any attribute can be eternal.
D) They claim that attributes cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that attributing knowledge and power to God does not imply they are effects, because subsisting in the essence does not require an efficient cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge and power, do you ever subconsciously imagine them as separate from His essence, like add-ons? How does that affect your trust in His perfect knowledge and power being eternal and unchanging? Commit today to affirming that His attributes are not caused, but are inherently part of who He is.
?
Ghazali points out that the philosophers' argument is 'mere intimidation by making the wording ugly.' Are there terms or labels in your own life—'unlucky', 'failure', 'too sinful'—that have intimidated you away from the truth of God's mercy and power? Which label will you reject today based on the truth you learned here?
?
If you can accept that God exists without a cause, can you also accept that His attributes exist without a cause? What does that demand of your understanding of God? Will you let that truth reshape how you rely on His knowledge and power in your daily struggles?

Refuting the Claim that Attributes Imply Need
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that attributes imply need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that divine attributes are not a sign of need but of perfection, and how the philosophers' objection is based on a linguistic confusion.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not external additions causing need; they are the very content of God's perfection.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles a central philosophical objection: if God has attributes, He would be dependent on them, compromising His absolute independence. Ghazali dismisses this as mere wordplay, showing that attributes of perfection are inseparable from the essence of the Perfect One. Let's see how he turns the philosophers' logic against them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' objection
The philosophers claim that if God has attributes, He would be in need of them, contradicting His absolute independence. Read the opening sentence of the passage to see their argument.
Notice how they equate having attributes with needing them.
2
Read Ghazali's initial dismissal
Ghazali immediately calls this objection 'flimsy verbiage.' Why does he think it is weak?
3
Grasp Ghazali's key insight
Attributes of perfection are not separate from the essence; they are constitutive of what it means to be perfect. Ghazali argues that God is eternally perfect through knowledge, power, and life, so calling that 'need' is a misuse of language.
Focus on the phrase 'صفات الكمال لا تباين ذات الكامل'.
4
Analyze the analogy
Ghazali gives an analogy: saying the perfect one needs perfection is contradictory. Instead, perfection means that perfection is inherent in the essence. Similarly, independence means the essence possesses attributes that negate need.
5
Conclude the refutation
Ghazali ends by challenging the philosophers: how can they deny attributes that establish divinity based on such linguistic confusions? Reflect on the strength of his argument.
This step ties the lesson together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما هو لوا بتقبيح العبارة من وجه آخر، فقالوا: هذا يؤدى إلى أن يكون الأول محتاجاً إلى هذه الصفات، فلا يكون غنياً مطلقاً إذا الغنى المطلق من لا يحتاج إلى غير ذاته

وهذا كلام لفظى فى غاية الركاكة، فإن صفات الكمال لا تباين ذات الكامل، حتى يقال: إنه محتاج إلى غيره، فإذا كان لم يزل ولا يزال كاملاً بالعلم والقدرة والحياة، فكيف يكون محتاجاً؟! أو كيف يجوز أن يعبر عن ملازمة الكمال بالحاجة، وهو كقول القائل: الكامل من لا يحتاج إلى كمال، فالمحتاج إلى وجود صفات الكمال لذاته، ناقص، فيقال: لا معنى لكونه كاملاً إلا الكمال لذاته، فكذلك لا معنى لكونه غنياً، إلا وجود وجود الصفات المنافية للحاجة، لذاته، فكيف تنكرون صفات الكمال التي بها الإلهية بمثل هذه التخيلات اللفظية
And perhaps they twisted [the meaning] by ugly wording from another angle, and said: 'This leads to the First being in need of these attributes, so He would not be absolutely independent, for absolute independence is that which does not need anything other than its essence.' This is verbiage of the utmost flimsiness, for attributes of perfection are not separate from the essence of the perfect one such that it is said that He is in need of something other than Himself. Since He has always been and always will be perfect through knowledge, power, and life, how could He be in need?! Or how is it permissible to express the concomitance of perfection as need? It is like someone who says: 'The perfect one is he who does not need perfection.' So the one who needs the existence of the attributes of perfection for his essence is deficient. Then it is said: There is no meaning to 'perfect' except perfection for His essence; similarly, there is no meaning to 'independent' except the existence of attributes that negate need for His essence. So how can you deny the attributes of perfection through which divinity is established, by such verbiage?
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' objection that if God has attributes, He would be needful of them, contradicting His absolute independence (ghina). Ghazali calls this argument flimsy verbiage. He argues that attributes of perfection are not external to the perfect being; they are constitutive of perfection itself. God is eternally perfect through knowledge, power, and life; therefore, He is not in need of them in the sense of lacking them. To call the concomitance of perfection 'need' is like saying the perfect one needs perfection – which is a contradiction. Rather, 'perfect' means that perfection is inherent in His essence, and 'independent' means that His essence possesses attributes that negate any lack. The philosophers' objection is based on a linguistic confusion between 'need' as a lack and the eternal possession of perfections.

Key Terms

محض
صفات الكمال
ذات
احتياج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute in God would imply a need or lack.
Correction: Attributes of perfection are not signs of deficiency; they are constitutive of perfection itself. God's eternal possession of knowledge, power, and life is not a need but the definition of His perfection.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali refute the claim that God's attributes imply need?
A) He says God can be perfect without attributes.
B) He argues that attributes of perfection are not separate from the essence; they are its perfection, so there is no need.
C) He claims that need only applies to contingent beings.
D) He says attributes are not real but only mental concepts.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that calling the presence of perfections 'need' is a linguistic confusion; divine attributes are the essence of perfection and do not indicate any lack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you envision a being so simple that He lacks attributes, or a being whose perfection requires them? How does your mental image align with Ghazali's argument? Take a moment to correct any hidden assumption that attributes diminish God's oneness, and commit to praising Him by His perfect names.
?
If someone told you that believing in God's attributes makes Him dependent, would you be able to refute it using the very wording of this passage? Are you ready to defend this truth? Reflect on whether you have truly internalized that God's attributes are not additions but His very perfection, and ask Allah to deepen your conviction.
?
The philosophers accused God of need because of His attributes. Have you ever unintentionally accused God of a limitation in your heart—perhaps by thinking He 'must' do something or that His mercy is constrained? Let this lesson remind you that His every attribute is perfect and without need. Repent for any thought that reduces His majesty.

Refuting the Composition Argument
Ghazali refutes the composition argument and exposes the philosophers' inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the argument that attributes imply composition and need for a composer, and his exposure of the philosophers' inconsistency.

Core Idea
An eternal qualified existent does not need a cause for its composition.

This passage tackles a central objection against affirming divine attributes: that they imply composition and hence need a composer. Ghazali responds with a powerful analogy, showing that the same logic would require an originator for God's existence. He then exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' own position regarding God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's analogy
Begin by reading the objection that essence plus attribute equals composition requiring a composer. Then note Ghazali's counter: that principle is like saying every existent needs an originator.
2
Understand the resolution
Ghazali argues that just as the First's existence is eternal and uncaused, so too His being qualified is eternal and uncaused. The entire reality (essence, attributes, their inherence) is without cause.
This is the key move: the need for a composer is not self-evident for eternal realities.
3
Distinguish bodies from the Eternal
Notice why bodies are excluded: they are originated and inseparable from accidents. If one does not accept the origination of bodies, even the First could be a body.
This shows the composition argument does not apply to the eternal qualified reality.
4
Recognize the final inconsistency
Ghazali points out that the philosophers themselves admit God's knowledge is additional to His essence, yet they cannot reduce it. This forces a dilemma about whether God knows other than Himself, showing their inconsistency.
Ibn Sina's view that God knows all things implies a real distinction between essence and attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذا أثبتم ذاتاً وصفة، وحلولا للصفة بالذات، فهو تركيب، وكل تركيب يحتاج إلى مركب، ولذلك لم يجز أن يكون الأول جسما لأنه مركب. قلنا: قول القائل: كل تركيب يحتاج إلى مركب، كقوله كل موجود يحتاج إلى موجد، فيقال له: الأول موجود قديم لا علة له ولا موجد، فكذلك يقال: هو موصوف قديم، ولا علة لذاته، ولا لصفاته، ولا لقيام صفته بذاته، بل الكل قديم بلا علة، وأما الجسم فإنما لم يجز أن يكون هو الأول لأنه حادث، من حيث إنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث، ومن لم يثبت له حدوث الجسم يلزمه أن يجوز أن تكون العلة الأولى جسما، كما سنلزمه لكم (١) من بعد

ثم إنهم لا يقدرون على رد جميع ما يثبتونه، إلى نفس الذات، فإنهم أثبتوا كونه عالماً» ويلزمهم أن يكون ذلك زائداً على مجرد الوجود، فيقال لهم: أتسلمون أن الأول يعلم غير ذاته؟! فمنهم من يسلم ذلك، ومنهم من قال لا يعلم إلا ذاته فأما الأول فهو الذي اختاره « ابن سينا »، فإنه زعم أنه يعلم الأشياء كلها
If it is said: 'When you affirm an essence and an attribute, and the indwelling of the attribute in the essence, that is composition, and every composition needs a composer, and therefore it is not permissible for the First to be a body because it is composed.' We say: The one who says 'every composition needs a composer' is like one who says 'every existent needs an originator.' So it is said to him: The First is an eternal existent with no cause and no originator; similarly, it is said: He is an eternal qualified [existent], and there is no cause for His essence, nor for His attributes, nor for the subsistence of His attribute in His essence; rather, all is eternal without a cause. As for the body, it is not permissible that it be the First only because it is originated, by virtue of its not being free from accidents. And one who does not affirm the origination of the body is compelled to allow that the First Cause could be a body, as we will compel you (1) later.

Moreover, they are unable to reduce everything they affirm to the essence itself, for they affirm that He is knowing, and it necessarily follows for them that this is additional to mere existence. So it is said to them: 'Do you concede that the First knows other than His essence?' Some of them concede that, and some said He knows only His essence. As for the former, it is the view chosen by Ibn Sina, for he claimed that He knows all things.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the composition argument and a final note on the philosophers' inconsistency. The objection: affirming essence and attribute together constitutes composition, and every composition needs a composer, so God would be caused. Ghazali responds by analogy: just as the First's existence has no cause, so His being qualified also has no cause; the entire reality (essence, attributes, and their inherence) is eternal without cause. The difference with a body is that bodies are originated and inseparable from accidents; if one does not accept the origination of bodies, he could even consider the First to be a body. Ghazali then points out a further inconsistency: the philosophers affirm that God is knowing, which they admit is additional to mere existence, and they cannot reduce it to the essence. This leads to a dilemma: either God knows other than Himself (Ibn Sina's position) or He knows only Himself. This shows that the philosophers themselves are forced to accept a real distinction between essence and attribute, contradicting their own claim.

Key Terms
تركيب
مركب
قديم
حادث
حدوث الجسم
ذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition always implies need for an external composer.
Correction: Ghazali shows that eternal composition (essence + attributes) can be uncaused, just as existence can be uncaused.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the error in the argument that composition implies need for a composer?
A) Composition is impossible in God.
B) The argument conflates the need for a composer with the need for an efficient cause; but if the Eternal Qualified exists without a cause for its essence, it also exists without a cause for its attributes.
C) The First can be both essence and attribute without composition.
D) The philosophers admit that bodies are composed but deny it for God.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the composition argument by showing that eternal qualified existence requires no cause, and then exposes the philosophers' inconsistency over God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself assuming that anything with multiple aspects must have an external cause to hold it together? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in the uncaused eternal nature of Allah's attributes?
?
The philosophers argued that composition implies need for a composer. In your own life, do you seek to 'compose' your identity or character through external factors rather than relying on Allah's eternal attributes? What would change if you truly believed that Allah's perfections are uncaused?
?
Ghazali points out that the philosophers themselves cannot reduce God's knowledge to His essence. Reflect: Do you ever reduce your understanding of Allah to a single attribute, ignoring the fullness of His names and attributes? How does this lesson call you to a deeper humility before the complexity of the Divine?

Refuting the Claim That Attributes Imply Need and Composition
Ghazali refutes two objections against divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the charge that divine attributes imply either need or composition, by appealing to the eternal and uncaused nature of God.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not accidental additions but eternal perfections inherent to God's essence.

In this passage, Ghazali tackles two related objections: that affirming attributes of perfection makes God need them, and that having multiple attributes entails composition. He argues that both objections rest on applying temporal analogies to the Eternal. By understanding his refutation, you will see how kalam defends the coherence of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing it. Let Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two clear parts: first refuting the 'need' argument, then the 'composition' argument.
2
Identify the two objections and responses
Find where Ghazali presents the objection about need and his response. Then find where he presents the composition objection and his response. Mark them in the text.
The composition objection is introduced with 'فإن قيل'.
3
Examine the key analogy
Ghazali compares 'every composition needs a composer' to 'every existent needs a cause'. Why does he say both are false for the First?
The analogy highlights that general rules can fail for the unique, eternal First.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كلام لفظى فى غاية الركاكة ، فإن صفات الكمال لا تباين ذات الكامل ، حتى يقال : إنه محتاج إلى غيره ، فإذا كان لم يزل ولا يزال كاملاً بالعلم والقدرة والحياة ، فكيف يكون محتاجاً ؟ ! أو كيف يجوز أن يعبر عن ملازمة الكمال بالحاجة ، وهو كقول القائل : الكامل من لا يحتاج إلى كمال ، فالمحتاج إلى وجود صفات الكمال لذاته ، ناقص ، فيقال : لا معنى لكونه كاملاً إلا الكمال لذاته ، فكذلك لا معنى لكونه غنياً ، إلا وجود وجود الصفات المنافية للحاجة ، لذاته ، فكيف تنكرون صفات الكمال التي بها الإلهية بمثل هذه التخيلات اللفظية
فإن قيل : إذا أثبتم ذاتاً وصفة ، وحلولا للصفة بالذات ، فهو تركيب ، وكل تركيب يحتاج إلى مركب ، ولذلك لم يجز أن يكون الأول جسما لأنه مركب . قلنا : قول القائل : كل تركيب يحتاج إلى مركب ، كقوله كل موجود يحتاج إلى موجد ، فيقال له : الأول موجود قديم لا علة له ولا موجد ، فكذلك يقال : هو موصوف قديم ، ولا علة لذاته ، ولا لصفاته ، ولا لقيام صفته بذاته ، بل الكل قديم بلا علة ، وأما الجسم فإنما لم يجز أن يكون هو الأول لأنه حادث ، من حيث إنه لا يخلو عن الحوادث ، ومن لم يثبت له حدوث الجسم يلزمه أن يجوز أن تكون العلة الأولى جسما ، كما سنلزمه لكم من بعد
وكل مسلكهم في هذه المسألة تخييلات
This is a verbal argument of extreme weakness, for the attributes of perfection do not contrast with the essence of the Perfect One, so that it could be said: He is in need of another. If He has always been and will always be perfect with knowledge, power, and life, how could He be in need? Or how is it permissible to express the concomitant of perfection as need? It is like one who says: The perfect one is he who does not need perfection, so he who needs the existence of the attributes of perfection for his essence is deficient. So it is said: There is no meaning to His being perfect except perfection for His own essence, and likewise there is no meaning to His being self-sufficient except the existence of attributes that negate need, for His own essence. So how can you deny the attributes of perfection, by which divinity is constituted, through such verbal illusions? For if it is said: If you affirm an essence and an attribute, and the inherence of the attribute in the essence, that is composition, and every composition needs a composer; therefore it is not permissible for the First to be a body because it is composite. We say: The statement of one who says ‘every composition needs a composer’ is like his statement ‘every existent needs a cause of existence’. So it is said to him: The First is an existent eternal, having no cause and no cause of existence; similarly it is said: He is described as eternal, having no cause for His essence, nor for His attributes, nor for the subsistence of His attribute in His essence; rather, all are eternal without cause. As for the body, it is not permissible for it to be the First only because it is temporally originated, insofar as it is not free from temporally originated events; and whoever does not affirm the temporality of the body is compelled to allow that the First Cause could be a body, as we will compel you later. And all their approach in this matter is illusions.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two related claims: first, that affirming divine attributes implies that God is in need of those attributes, and second, that affirming attributes alongside the essence amounts to composition (tarkib) which requires a composer. Ghazali begins by dismissing the first claim as a weak verbal trick: the attributes of perfection are not separate from the Perfect One in a way that would imply need. If God has always been perfect with knowledge, power, and life, then He is not in need of them; rather, perfection is inherent to His essence. Similarly, self-sufficiency (ghina) does not mean lack of attributes, but the possession of attributes that negate need. Ghazali then presents the objection: if one affirms an essence and an attribute, and the attribute's inherence in the essence, that is composition, and every composition needs a composer. Therefore, the First cannot be composite like a body. Ghazali's answer draws an analogy: just as the statement 'every existent needs a cause of existence' is false for the eternal First, so too the statement 'every composition needs a composer' is false for the eternal First. The First is eternal and uncaused; His essence, attributes, and the inherence of attributes in essence are all eternal and without cause. As for bodies, they are considered composite because they are temporally originated and subject to events; but if one does not accept the temporality of bodies, one could logically allow the First to be a body. Ghazali concludes that all the philosophers' arguments on this point are mere illusions.

Key Terms
صفات الكمال
تركيب
مركب
حادث
قديم
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming multiple attributes implies composition and need for a composer.
Correction: Ghazali argues that this only applies to temporal composites; the eternal First is uncaused, so His attributes are uncaused and do not imply composition in the same sense.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's response to the claim that divine attributes entail composition (tarkib)?
A) He denies that God has any attributes.
B) He argues that attributes are not additional to the essence and therefore no composition occurs.
C) He says that the First is eternal and uncaused, so His attributes are likewise uncaused, breaking the analogy with temporal composites.
D) He says that only bodies are composite, but God is not a body, so the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the principle 'every composition needs a composer' does not apply to the eternal First, whose attributes are co-eternal and uncaused.
