The Dilemma of God's Knowledge of Others
Ghazali presents the dilemma that God's knowledge of others must be either identical to or distinct from His self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if God knows other things, that knowledge must be distinct from His self-knowledge, leading to multiplicity in the divine essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of others cannot be identical to His self-knowledge, proving multiplicity.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers who affirm that God knows things other than Himself. He presents a logical dilemma: either that knowledge is identical to His self-knowledge or distinct. If distinct, multiplicity follows. If identical, absurd consequences arise. This lesson examines the core argument and the criterion of separability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of Ghazali's challenge. Notice how he sets up the dilemma and uses the example of human knowledge.
2
Identify the initial question
Find where Ghazali asks the philosophers: 'Do you acknowledge that the First knows other than His essence?' This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the dilemma
Ghazali poses a binary choice: either God's knowledge of others is identical to His essence or it is other. Both options lead to problems: distinctness implies multiplicity, identity leads to absurdity.
The rule 'if it is other, you have affirmed multiplicity' is key.
4
Examine the criterion of separability
Ghazali argues that if two knowledges were identical, one could not be imagined without the other. Using human self-knowledge and knowledge of another, he shows they are distinguishable, proving they are distinct.
Apply the same reasoning to God: self-knowledge without knowledge of others is conceivable, so they are distinct.
5
Conclude with Ghazali's verdict
The passage ends with the conclusion that any philosopher who affirms God knows others has thereby affirmed multiplicity, which undermines divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم لا يقدرون على رد جميع ما يثبتونه ، إلى نفس الذات ، فإنهم أثبتوا كونه عالماً ويلزمهم أن يكون ذلك زائداً على مجرد الوجود ، فيقال لهم : أتسلمون أن الأول يعلم غير ذاته ؟ ! فمنهم من يسلم ذلك ، ومنهم من قال لا يعلم إلا ذاته فأما الأول فهو الذي اختاره « ابن سينا » ، فإنه زعم أنه يعلم الأشياء كلها بنوع كلى ، لا يدخل تحت الزمان ، ولا يعلم الجزئيات التي توجب تجدد الإحاطة بها تغيراً في ذات العالم .
فنقول : علم الأول بوجود كل الأنواع والأجناس التي لا نهاية لها علمه بنفسه ، أو غيره ؟ ! فإن قلتم : إنه غيره فقد أثبتم كثرة ، وتفضتم القاعدة ، وإن قلتم إنه عينه ، لم تتميزوا عمن يدعى أن علم الإنسان بغيره عين علمه بنفسه ، وعين ذاته ، ومن قال ذلك ، سفه في عقله وقيل : حد الشيء الواحد ، أن يستحيل في الوهم الجمع فيه بين النفى والإثبات ، فكذلك العلم بالشيء الواحد ، لما كان شيئاً واحداً ، استحال أن يتوهم في حال واحدة موجوداً معدوماً ، ولما لم يستحل في الوهم أن يقدر علم الإنسان بنفسه ، دون علمه بغيره ، قيل : إن علمه بغيره غير علمه بنفسه ، إذ لو كان هو هو ، لكان نفيه نفياً له ، وإثباته إثباتاً له ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون زيد موجوداً ، وزيد معدوماً ، في نفسه في حال واحدة ، ولا يستحيل مثل ذلك في العلم بالغير مع العلم بنفسه ، وكذا في علم الأول بذاته ، مع علمه بغيره ، إذ يمكن أن يتوهم أحدهما دون الآخر ، فهما إذن شيئان ، ولا يمكن أن يتوهم وجود أحدهما دون وجود ذاته ، فلو كان الكل كذلك ، لكان هذا التوهم محالا ، فكل من اعترف من الفلاسفة بأن الأول يعلم غير ذاته فقد أثبت كثرة لا محالة
فإن قيل : هو لا يعلم الغير بالقصد الأول ، بل يعلم ذاته مبدأ للكل ، فيلزمه العلم بالكل بالقصد الثانى ، إذ لا يمكن أن يعلم ذاته إلا مبدأ ، فإنه حقيقة ذاته ، ولا يمكن أن يعلم ذاته مبدأ لغيره ، إلا ويدخل الغير في علمه ، بطريق التضمن واللزوم ، ولا يبعد أن تكون لذاته لوازم ، وذلك لا يوجب كثرة في ماهية الذات ، وإنما يمتنع أن تكون فى نفس الذات كثرة .
Then they are unable to reduce all that they affirm to the essence itself, for they affirm that He is knowing, and they are obliged to hold that that is additional to mere existence. So it is said to them: Do you acknowledge that the First knows other than His essence? Some of them acknowledge that, and some say He knows only His essence. As for the first, it is what Ibn Sina chose, for he claimed that He knows all things in a universal way, not entering under time, and He does not know particulars that would necessitate a change in the knower's essence due to renewed encompassing. So we say: The knowledge of the First of the existence of all species and genera that have no end—is it His knowledge by His essence or other than it? If you say it is other, then you have affirmed multiplicity and broken the rule; and if you say it is identical to it, then you are not distinguished from one who claims that a human's knowledge of another is identical to his self-knowledge and to his essence. Whoever says that is foolish in his intellect, and it is said: The definition of a single thing is that it is impossible in estimation to combine negation and affirmation regarding it. Similarly, knowledge of a single thing, since it is a single thing, it is impossible to estimate it as existing and non-existing in a single state. And since it is not impossible in estimation to conceive of a human's self-knowledge without his knowledge of another, it is said that his knowledge of another is other than his self-knowledge; for if it were identical, then negating it would be negating it, and affirming it would be affirming it, since it is impossible that Zayd exists and Zayd does not exist in himself in one state. But such is not impossible regarding knowledge of another with self-knowledge. Likewise in the knowledge of the First of His essence, with His knowledge of another, since it is possible to estimate one without the other, so they are two things. And it is not possible to estimate existence without the existence of His essence; if everything were like that, this estimation would be impossible. So whoever among the philosophers acknowledges that the First knows other than His essence has affirmed multiplicity without doubt. For if it is said: He does not know the other with primary intention, but He knows His essence as the principle of everything, so knowledge of everything follows by secondary intention, since He cannot know His essence except as a principle, for that is the reality of His essence; and He cannot know His essence as principle of other unless the other enters into His knowledge by way of implication and concomitance; and it is not improbable that His essence has concomitants, and that does not necessitate multiplicity in the quiddity of the essence; rather, multiplicity is only prohibited in the essence itself.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument that if God knows things other than Himself, then that knowledge must be distinct from His self-knowledge, thereby entailing multiplicity (kathra) in the divine essence—a conclusion the philosophers seek to avoid. Ghazali begins by noting that the philosophers who affirm God's knowledge cannot reduce all attributes to the essence; they are forced to admit that knowledge is additional to mere existence. He then asks them directly: Do you accept that the First knows other than His essence? Some philosophers (like Ibn Sina) say yes, some say no. Ibn Sina claims God knows all things universally, not temporally, and does not know particulars because that would cause change. Ghazali then poses a dilemma: Is God's knowledge of all species and genera identical to His essence or other? If other, multiplicity follows; if identical, then one could absurdly claim that a human's knowledge of another is identical to his self-knowledge. Ghazali explains the criterion: a single thing cannot be imagined both affirmed and denied in one state. Since human self-knowledge can be imagined without knowledge of another, they are distinct. By analogy, God's self-knowledge can be imagined without His knowledge of other things—since one can conceive of His essence alone—so they are two different things. Hence, affirming that God knows others forces multiplicity. Finally, Ghazali presents an objection: God knows others only by secondary intention, as necessary concomitants of knowing Himself as the principle of all. Ghazali implicitly rejects this, but the passage ends with the objection's claim that concomitants do not introduce multiplicity in the quiddity of the essence.

Key Terms
علم
ذات
كثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that identical knowledge can refer to two different objects without distinction.
Correction: Ghazali argues that knowledges are distinguished by their objects and by the possibility of imagining one without the other; if they were identical, this separation would be impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why must God's knowledge of other things be distinct from His self-knowledge?
A) Because God's knowledge is universal and not particular.
B) Because self-knowledge and knowledge of another can be imagined separately, showing they are not identical.
C) Because the philosophers unanimously say so.
D) Because God's essence is simple and cannot have any distinctions.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that any knowledge of other beings distinct from self-knowledge entails multiplicity in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every detail of your life, including your secret thoughts? How does this affect the way you live when no one is watching?
?
If Ghazali is right that God's knowledge of you is distinct from His self-knowledge, does that make you feel more accountable or more distant from Him? Why?
?
You can conceive of God existing without knowledge of others—yet He still knows you out of His will. How does that make you feel about the value of your existence in His sight? What change in your daily choices does this demand?

The Objection: Primary and Secondary Intention
Ghazali presents the philosophers' objection that God knows others only by secondary intention as a concomitant of self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that God knows others only by secondary intention as a necessary concomitant of knowing Himself, and Ghazali's implicit rejection of this as a way to avoid multiplicity.

Core Idea
Knowing others as concomitants of self-knowledge still implies multiplicity.

After establishing that knowledge of others must be distinct, Ghazali considers an objection: God knows others only indirectly, as necessary implications of His self-knowledge. The objection claims this does not introduce multiplicity in the quiddity of the essence. This lesson examines that objection and shows how Ghazali's earlier argument undermines it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the distinction between primary intention (direct) and secondary intention (indirect).
2
Understand primary vs. secondary intention
The objection argues that God's primary object of knowledge is His own essence, and knowing others is a necessary consequence (secondary intention) of knowing Himself as the principle of all things.
Secondary intention means the knowledge of others follows automatically, without a separate act.
3
Analyze the claim about concomitants
The objection states that God's essence can have necessary concomitants (e.g., being a principle) that do not add multiplicity to the essence itself. This attempts to preserve divine simplicity.
4
Evaluate against Ghazali's earlier argument
Recall Ghazali's separability test: if self-knowledge can be imagined without knowledge of others, they are distinct. Even if the knowledge of others is only a concomitant, it is still a distinct mental content, thus multiplicity remains.
Ghazali does not explicitly rebut this objection in the passage, but his earlier reasoning implies rejection.

God's Knowledge of Others and the Problem of Multiplicity
Ghazali argues that if God knows anything besides Himself, multiplicity in the essence is unavoidable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that if God knows anything other than Himself, multiplicity in the divine essence is inevitable.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of the world cannot be identical to His essence without causing multiplicity.

Ghazali confronts the philosophers' claim that God knows all things in a universal, timeless manner. He argues that this very knowledge, if it includes anything besides His essence, introduces multiplicity into the divine nature. He presents a dilemma and a logical criterion to prove that such knowledge cannot be one with the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two possibilities Ghazali presents for God's knowledge of species and genera.
The dilemma is: either God's knowledge is identical to His essence or it is other.
2
Understand the criterion for distinguishing two things
Ghazali uses the criterion that two things are distinct if one can be imagined without the other. Note how he applies this to human knowledge.
He argues that human self-knowledge can be imagined without knowledge of others, so they are distinct.
3
Apply the criterion to God and draw the conclusion
Apply the same reasoning to God: since God's self-knowledge can be imagined without His knowledge of other things, they are distinct, entailing multiplicity. Reflect on why this matters.
The conclusion is that any philosopher who admits God knows other than Himself must admit multiplicity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنوع كلى، لا يدخل تحت الزمان، ولا يعلم الجزئيات التي توجب تجدد الإحاطة بها تغيراً في ذات العالم. فنقول: علم الأول بوجود كل الأنواع والأجناس التي لا نهاية لها علمه بنفسه، أو غيره؟ فإن قلتم: إنه غيره فقد أثبتم كثرة، وتفضتم [؟] القاعدة، وإن قلتم إنه عينه، لم تتميزوا عمن يدعى أن علم الإنسان بغيره عين علمه بنفسه، وعين ذاته، ومن قال ذلك سفه في عقله وقيل: حد الشيء الواحد أن يستحيل في الوهم الجمع فيه بين النفي والإثبات، فالعلم بالشيء الواحد لما كان شيئاً واحداً استحال أن يتوهم في حال واحدة موجوداً معدوماً، ولما لم يستحل في الوهم أن يقدر علم الإنسان بنفسه دون علمه بغيره، قيل: إن علمه بغيره غير علمه بنفسه، إذ لو كان هو هو، لكان نفيه نفياً له وإثباته إثباتاً له، إذ يستحيل أن يكون زيد موجوداً وزيد معدوماً نفسه في حال واحدة، ولا يستحيل مثل ذلك في العلم بالغير مع العلم بنفسه، وكذا في علم الأول بذاته مع علمه بغيره، إذ يمكن أن يتوهم أحدهما دون الآخر، فهما إذن شيئان، ولا يمكن أن يتوهم وجود دون وجود ذاته، فلو كان الكل كذلك لكان هذا التوهم محالا، فكل من اعترف من الفلاسفة بأن الأول يعلم غير ذاته فقد أثبت كثرة لا محالة.
By a universal mode, not entering under time, and He does not know the particulars that necessitate renewal of encompassment in a change in the essence of the world. We say: The First's knowledge of the existence of all the species and genera that have no end—is it His essence or other? If you say it is other, you have affirmed multiplicity and [؟] the rule. If you say it is identical, you are not distinguished from one who claims that a human's knowledge of other is identical to his knowledge of himself and his essence. Whoever said that has foolishness in his intellect, and it was said: The definition of a single thing is that it is impossible in estimation to combine negation and affirmation in it. So knowledge of a single thing, since it is one thing, is impossible to be imagined in a single state as existing and non-existent. Since it is not impossible in estimation to suppose a human's knowledge of himself without his knowledge of other, it was said: his knowledge of other is not his knowledge of himself, for if it were the same, its negation would be a negation of it and its affirmation an affirmation of it, since it is impossible for Zayd to exist and Zayd to be non-existent himself in a single state, and such is not impossible in the case of knowledge of other with knowledge of himself. Likewise in the First's knowledge of His essence with His knowledge of other, since it is possible to imagine one of them without the other, so they are two things. And it is not possible to imagine existence without the existence of His essence. So if everything were like that, this imagination would be impossible. Thus every philosopher who admits that the First knows other than His essence has indeed affirmed multiplicity inevitably.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a proof that if God knows anything other than Himself, multiplicity (kathra) must follow in His essence. Ghazali first criticizes the philosophers' claim that God knows only universals in a timeless manner and does not know changing particulars. He then poses a dilemma: God's knowledge of all species and genera is either identical to His essence or something else. If it is other, then multiplicity is admitted. If it is identical, then it would be like saying a human's knowledge of other things is identical to his self-knowledge—an absurdity. Ghazali explains that a single thing cannot be both affirmed and negated in the same state, and since one can imagine knowledge of self without knowledge of other, the two knowledges are distinct. Applying this to God, since one can imagine God's self-knowledge without His knowledge of other things, they are two distinct things, entailing multiplicity. Therefore, any philosopher who says God knows other than Himself must admit multiplicity.

Key Terms
الكثرة
العلم بالذات
العلم بالغير
الوهم
النفي والإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies all divine knowledge of creation.
Correction: Ghazali only denies that such knowledge is identical to the essence without causing multiplicity; he does not deny that God knows creation, but he argues that any such knowledge introduces multiplicity into the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the claim that God's knowledge of species is 'other' than His essence lead to multiplicity?
A) Because 'other' means a separate entity.
B) Because it implies a distinction between knowledge and essence, which is multiplicity.
C) Because it introduces temporal change.
D) Because it denies God's omniscience.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that any claim that God knows anything other than Himself inevitably introduces multiplicity into the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God knows you completely—your thoughts, actions, and secrets. According to Ghazali, this knowledge implies multiplicity in God. Does this disturb your understanding of tawhid? How do you personally reconcile God's perfect oneness with His intimate knowledge of your life? Will you take time today to meditate on this tension and ask God for deeper conviction?
?
If Ghazali's argument is correct, and God's knowledge of you is not His essence but something added, does that make you feel that God is less close? Or does it magnify His greatness? Examine your heart: do you prefer a God whose knowledge is identical to Himself (so that He is maximally simple) or a God whose knowledge of you is a distinct act? Which gives you more comfort, and why? Commit to exploring one more scholarly opinion on this issue within the next week.
?
When you sin, do you think of God as knowing about it while remaining utterly one? How does this passage challenge you to think more carefully about God's attributes? Before you sleep tonight, write down one way this lesson should change how you speak about God's knowledge in your daily conversations.

Refuting Indirect Knowledge: The Relation Problem
Ghazali presents philosophers' objection and his first refutation focusing on relational nature of sourcehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that claiming God knows Himself as a source introduces a relational attribute additional to the essence, thereby entailing multiplicity.

Core Idea
Knowing God as 'source' adds a relation—this breaks divine simplicity.

The philosophers attempt to safeguard divine simplicity by claiming God does not know other things directly but only as a necessary consequence of self-knowledge. Ghazali's first counterattack targets this very claim: even calling God 'source' involves a relational concept that is distinct from the essence, undermining the claim of absolute oneness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the philosophers' objection carefully. They argue that God knows only Himself, and knowledge of other things follows by 'second intention' because He knows Himself as the source of all.
Notice the key terms: 'first intention' and 'second intention'.
2
Identify Ghazali's first answer
Find where Ghazali begins his answer. He calls the claim arbitrary and distinguishes between knowing existence and knowing as a source.
3
Understand the relation argument
Ghazali argues that 'sourcehood' (mabda'iyya) is a relation (idafa) added to the essence. If it were not distinct, the essence would have multiple aspects. Since a relation is other than the essence, knowing the essence as source already introduces multiplicity.
He uses the analogy of a human knowing himself without knowing he is an effect.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutes the philosophers' premise that God's self-knowledge automatically includes knowledge of all things without multiplicity.
This shows that even their starting point is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هو لا يعلم الغير بالقصد الأول، بل يعلم ذاته مبدأ للكل، فيلزمه العلم بالكل بالقصد الثاني، إذ لا يمكن أن يعلم ذاته إلا مبدأ، فإنه حقيقة ذاته، ولا يمكن أن يعلم ذاته مبدأ لغيره، إلا ويدخل الغير في علمه، بطريق التضمن واللزوم، ولا يبعد أن تكون لذاته لوازم، وذلك لا يوجب كثرة في ماهية الذات، وإنما يمتنع أن تكون في نفس الذات كثرة. والجواب من وجوه: الأول، أن قولكم: إنه يعلم ذاته مبدأ، تحكم، بل ينبغي أن يعلم وجود ذاته فقط، فأما العلم بكونه مبدأ، فيزيد على العلم بالوجود، لأن المبدئية إضافة إلى الذات، ويجوز أن يعلم الذات، ولا يعلم إضافته؛ ولو لم تكن المبدئية إضافة، لتكثرت ذاته، وكان له وجود ومبدئية، وهما شيئان، وكما يجوز أن يعلم الإنسان ذاته، ولا يعلم كونه معلولا، إلى أن يعلم [؟]، لأن كونه معلولا إضافة له إلى علته، فكذلك كونه علة إضافة له إلى معلوله، فالإلزام قائم في مجرد قولهم: إنه يعلم كونه مبدأ، إذ فيه علم بالذات، وبالمبدئية، وهو الإضافة، والإضافة غير الذات، والعلم بالإضافة غير العلم بالذات، أنه يمكن أن يتوهم العلم بالذات، دون العلم بالمبدئية بالدليل الذي ذكرناه وهو أنه يمكن أن يتوهم العلم بالذات دون العلم بالمبدئية، ولا يمكن أن يتوهم العلم بالذات دون العلم بالذات، لأن الذات واحدة. الوجه الثانى هو أن قولكم: إن الكل معلوم له بالقصد الثانى، كلام غير معقول، فإنه مهما كان علمه محيطاً بغيره، كما يحيط بذاته، كان له معلومان متغايران، وكان له علم بهما، وتعدد المعلوم وتغايره، يوجب تعدد العلم، إذ يقبل أحد المعلومين الفصل عن الآخر فى الوهم، فلايكون العلم بأحدهما عين العلم بالآخر، إذ لو كان العلم بأحدهما عين العلم بالآخر، لتعذر تقدير وجود أحدهما دون الآخر، وليس ثم آخر، مهما كان الكل واحداً، فهذا لا يختلف بأن يعبر عنه بالقصد الثاني ثم ليت شعرى، كيف يقدم على نفى الكثرة من يقول: إنه لا يعزب عن علمه مثقال ذرة فى السموات ولا فى الأرض، إلا أنه يعرف الكل بنوع كلى؛ والكليات المعلومة له لا تتناهى، فيكون العلم المتعلق بها – مع كثرتها وتغايرها - واحداً من كل وجه وقد خالف « ابن سينا » فى هذا غيره من الفلاسفة، الذين ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه، احترازاً عن لزوم الكثرة فكيف شاركهم فى نفى الكثرة
If it is said: He does not know the other with the first intention, but He knows His essence as the source of all, so knowledge of all follows by the second intention, since He cannot know His essence except as a source, for that is the reality of His essence, and He cannot know His essence as a source for other unless the other enters His knowledge by way of inclusion and entailment, and it is not unlikely that His essence has necessary concomitants, and that does not necessitate multiplicity in the quiddity of the essence, but multiplicity is impossible in the essence itself. The answer has several aspects: First, that your statement that He knows His essence as a source is arbitrary; rather, He should only know the existence of His essence. As for knowledge of His being a source, it adds to knowledge of existence, because sourcehood (al-mabda'iyya) is a relation (idafa) to the essence, and it is possible to know the essence without knowing its relation. And if sourcehood were not a relation, His essence would be multiple, having existence and sourcehood, which are two things. And just as it is possible for a human to know his essence without knowing that he is an effect, until he knows [?], because his being an effect is a relation to his cause, so also his being a cause is a relation to his effect. So the objection stands on their mere statement that He knows His being a source, for in it there is knowledge of the essence and of sourcehood, which is the relation, and the relation is other than the essence, and knowledge of the relation is other than knowledge of the essence, because it is possible to imagine knowledge of the essence without knowledge of sourcehood by the proof we mentioned, namely that it is possible to imagine knowledge of the essence without knowledge of sourcehood, but it is impossible to imagine knowledge of the essence without knowledge of the essence, because the essence is one. The second aspect: That your statement that all is known to Him by the second intention is unintelligible speech. For whenever His knowledge encompasses other, as it encompasses His essence, He has two distinct known things and has knowledge of them, and the multiplicity and distinctness of the known necessitates multiplicity of knowledge, since one of the two known things admits of separation from the other in estimation, so knowledge of one is not identical to knowledge of the other. For if knowledge of one were identical to knowledge of the other, it would be impossible to suppose the existence of one without the other, and there is no 'other' when all is one. This does not differ by expressing it as 'second intention.' Then how I wish I knew: how can one who says that not a speck's weight in the heavens or on earth escapes His knowledge, yet He knows all by a universal mode, and the known universals are infinite, so that the knowledge related to them—despite their multiplicity and diversity—is one from every aspect, how can such a one proceed to deny multiplicity? And Ibn Sina disagreed with other philosophers who held that He knows only Himself, as a precaution against the entailment of multiplicity. So how did he join them in denying multiplicity?
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage contains the philosophers' objection and Ghazali's two-fold refutation. The objection attempts to avoid multiplicity by claiming that God does not know other things primarily (bi'l-qasd al-awwal), but only knows Himself as the source (mabda') of all, and through that necessary relation He knows all things by 'second intention' (bi'l-qasd al-thani). Ghazali first answers by calling this arbitrary: God's knowledge of His own existence does not automatically include knowledge of His being a source, since sourcehood is a relation (idafa) distinct from the essence. If it were not distinct, the essence would become multiple. He uses an analogy: a human may know himself without knowing that he is an effect, until he knows (the cause); similarly, God's being a cause is a relation additional to His essence. Thus even the claim that He knows Himself as a source introduces multiplicity because it adds the relational aspect. The second answer attacks the concept of 'second intention' as unintelligible: if God knows other things, they are distinct objects of knowledge, and distinct objects require distinct acts of knowledge. The fact that one can be imagined without the other proves they are not identical. Ghazali then points out the inconsistency among philosophers: some (like Ibn Sina) claim that God knows all things universally, yet also deny multiplicity, while other philosophers avoided this by saying God knows only Himself. He questions how Ibn Sina can join in denying multiplicity while affirming knowledge of infinite universals.

Key Terms
القصد الأول
المبدئية
الإضافة
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies God knows anything other than Himself.
Correction: Ghazali affirms divine omniscience but rejects the philosophical explanation that tries to reconcile it with simplicity by introducing relational concepts that actually entail multiplicity.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ghazali's first answer to the philosophers' objection?
A) God cannot know Himself as source without knowing His essence.
B) 'Sourcehood' is a relation additional to the essence, so knowing it introduces multiplicity.
C) The objection is invalid because it relies on the concept of 'second intention'.
D) God's knowledge is entirely simple and cannot include relations.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first refutation shows that even the philosophers' own description of God's knowledge entails multiplicity because 'sourcehood' is a relational attribute distinct from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as your Creator and Sustainer, do you treat these roles as separate 'parts' of God, or do you remember that they are mere relations that do not add to His essence? How can you train your heart to avoid dividing God in your mind?
?
The philosophers tried to protect divine simplicity but ended up introducing relations. In your own faith, are there areas where you hold onto neat theological formulas that actually distance you from the living reality of God? What would it cost you to let go of them?
?
If knowing God as source adds a relation, then all your perceptions of God's attributes are filtered through your own limited human perspective. Does this humble you? Will you commit today to seeking God as He is, not as you conceptualize Him, even if that means admitting your intellectual constructs fall short?

The Second Intention Refuted: Knowledge of Many Requires Many Knowings
Ghazali presents his second refutation attacking the concept of second intention, and critiques Ibn Sina's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that the concept of 'second intention' cannot rescue divine simplicity, and his critique of Ibn Sina's inconsistent position.

Core Idea
Knowing many distinct things requires distinct acts of knowledge—oneness cannot be preserved by renaming.

Having shown that even the first step introduces a relation, Ghazali now dismantles the core evasion: the notion of 'second intention'. He argues that if God knows multiple objects, those objects are distinct and separable in thought, necessitating multiple acts of knowledge. He then exposes an inconsistency in Ibn Sina's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect
Read Ghazali's second answer starting with 'الوجه الثانى' He attacks the concept of 'second intention' directly.
Pay attention to the logical argument about distinct known objects.
2
Understand the argument
Ghazali says: if God knows other things, they are distinct known objects. Distinct objects can be separated in estimation (e.g., one can imagine knowing one without the other). Therefore, knowledge of one is not identical to knowledge of the other. Hence, multiple objects require multiple acts of knowledge.
This refutes the idea that 'second intention' makes knowledge one.
3
Identify the critique of Ibn Sina
Ghazali points out that Ibn Sina claimed God knows all things by a universal mode, yet also denied multiplicity. He questions how one can affirm infinite known universals while insisting on absolute oneness.
Note that other philosophers avoided this by saying God knows only Himself.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument shows the impossibility of reconciling omniscience with philosophical simplicity without resorting to incoherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if sourcehood were not a relation, His essence would be multiple, having existence and sourcehood, which are two things. And just as it is possible for a human to know his essence without knowing that he is an effect, until he knows [?], because his being an effect is a relation to his cause, so also his being a cause is a relation to his effect. So the objection stands on their mere statement that He knows His being a source, for in it there is knowledge of the essence and of sourcehood, which is the relation, and the relation is other than the essence, and knowledge of the relation is other than knowledge of the essence, because it is possible to imagine knowledge of the essence without knowledge of sourcehood by the proof we mentioned, namely that it is possible to imagine knowledge of the essence without knowledge of sourcehood, but it is impossible to imagine knowledge of the essence without knowledge of the essence, because the essence is one. The second aspect: That your statement that all is known to Him by the second intention is unintelligible speech. Commentary
This passage contains the philosophers' objection and Ghazali's two-fold refutation. The objection attempts to avoid multiplicity by claiming that God does not know other things primarily (bi'l-qasd al-awwal), but only knows Himself as the source (mabda') of all, and through that necessary relation He knows all things by 'second intention' (bi'l-qasd al-thani). First Argument: Knowledge of a Relation is Additional
Ghazali presents the first argument: knowing God as principle involves a relation, causing multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that knowledge of a relation (such as being a principle) is additional to knowledge of the essence, implying multiplicity in divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge of a relation is never the same as knowledge of the essence.

Ghazali begins his refutation of the philosophers by attacking their claim that God knows Himself as the principle of all things. He argues that 'being a principle' is a relation, and knowledge of a relation is distinct from knowledge of the essence itself. This distinction implies multiplicity, which the philosophers wish to avoid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the garbled portion near the end; the meaning is clear from context.
2
Identify the key claim and the analogy
Find where Ghazali states that principiality is a relation, and then find the analogy with a human knowing his essence without knowing he is an effect.
The analogy is crucial for understanding the argument.
3
Trace the logical entailment
Work through the argument step by step: if principiality is a relation, then knowledge of it is additional to knowledge of the essence, leading to multiplicity in knowledge.
This directly challenges the philosophers' claim of unitary knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب من وجوه : الأول ، أن قولكم : إنه يعلم ذاته مبدأ ، تحكم ، بل ينبغي أن يعلم وجود ذاته فقط ، فأما العلم بكونه مبدأ ، فيزيد على العلم بالوجود ، لأن المبدئية إضافة إلى الذات ، ويجوز أن يعلم الذات ، ولا يعلم إضافته ؛ ولو لم تكن المبدئية إضافة ، لتكثرت ذاته ، وكان له وجود ومبدئية ، وهما شيئان ، وكما يجوز أن يعلم الإنسان ذاته ، ولا يعلم كونه معلولا ، إلى أن يعلم ، لأن كونه معلولا إضافة له إلى علته ، فكذلك كونه علة إضافة له إلى معلوله ، فالإلزام قائم في مجرد قولهم : إنه يعلم كونه مبدأ ، إذ فيه علم بالذات ، وبالمبدئية ، وهو الإضافة ، والإضافة غير الذات ، والعلم بالإضافة غير العلم بالذات ، أنه يمكن أن يتوهم العلم بالذات ، دون العلم بالمبدئية بالدليل الذي ذكرناه وهو أنه يمكن أن يتوهم العلم [با] ولا يمكن أن يتوهم العلم بالذات ، دون العلم بالذات ، لأن الذات واحدة .
And the answer is from several aspects: First, that your statement that He knows His essence as a principle is arbitrary; rather, He should know only the existence of His essence. As for the knowledge of His being a principle, it increases over knowledge of existence, because principiality (al-mabda'iyya) is a relation (idaafa) to the essence. It is possible that one knows the essence but does not know its relation. And if principiality were not a relation, His essence would be multiple, and He would have existence and principiality, which are two things. And just as it is possible for a human to know his own essence but not know his being an effect until he knows, because his being an effect is a relation to his cause, so too His being a cause is a relation to His effect. So the entailment stands in the mere saying that He knows His being a principle, for it involves knowledge of the essence and knowledge of principiality, which is the relation. And the relation is other than the essence, and knowledge of the relation is other than knowledge of the essence. Indeed, it is possible to conceive of knowledge of the essence without knowledge of principiality, by the proof we mentioned, which is that it is possible to conceive of knowledge [of ?] and it is not possible to conceive of knowledge of the essence without knowledge of the essence, because the essence is one.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's first argument against the philosophers who claim that God knows His essence as a principle (mabda') of all things. Ghazali argues that this claim is arbitrary (tahakkum), for God's knowledge of His essence ought to be only of its existence, not of its being a principle. The knowledge of being a principle is an addition over knowledge of existence, because 'being a principle' is a relation (idaafa) to something else. If it were not a relation, then the essence would become multiple, having both existence and principiality as separate attributes. Ghazali uses an analogy: a human can know his own essence without knowing that he is an effect of a cause, because being an effect is a relation. Similarly, God's being a cause is a relation to His effect. Therefore, when the philosophers say God knows His being a principle, that entails knowledge of both the essence and the relation, and since the relation is other than the essence, the knowledge of the relation is other than the knowledge of the essence. This shows multiplicity in knowledge, which they deny. The argument concludes that the essence alone is one, but knowledge of it plus the relation is multiple.

Key Terms
مبدأ (mabda')
إضافة (idaafa)
المبدئية (al-mabda'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge of a relation is identical to knowledge of the essence.
Correction: Knowledge of a relation, such as 'being a principle,' is conceptually distinct from knowledge of the essence itself, as the passage argues.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ghazali's first argument?
A) God knows only His essence.
B) Knowledge of a relation is additional to knowledge of the essence, causing multiplicity.
C) God's knowledge is unitary and cannot be multiple.
D) The philosophers are correct.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first argument demonstrates that knowledge of a relation is necessarily additional to knowledge of the essence, implying multiplicity in divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you mostly focus on what He is to you—your Creator, Provider, Guide—rather than on His very essence? Ghazali argues that those are relations. Are you content knowing only the relations, or do you yearn for the essence itself? What would it take for you to seek knowledge of God beyond your own needs?
?
This lesson challenges the idea that knowing God as a principle is identical to knowing His essence. In your own life, when you say 'I know God', are you really just knowing your own limited experiences of Him? Can you point to a moment when you mistook a relational attribute for the fullness of who He is? How will you guard against reducing God to your own perceptions?
?
Ghazali says knowledge of a relation is other than knowledge of the essence. Consider your own knowledge of yourself: do you know yourself more by your relationships (son, parent, employee) than your own essence? If so, how much more true is that for your knowledge of God? What might it mean to know God without any relation—just as He is in Himself? Can you even imagine it? And if not, does that humble you? How will you respond to the limitation of your own understanding?

Refuting 'Secondary Intention' Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim about secondary intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the concept of 'secondary intention' does not resolve the problem of multiplicity in God's knowledge, and how Ibn Sina's position is contradictory.

Core Idea
The claim that God knows all things through a single concept is self-contradictory.

Ghazali presents a powerful critique of the philosophers' attempt to preserve God's unity while affirming His knowledge of all things. He focuses on the term 'secondary intention' (al-qasd al-thaani), which the philosophers used to explain how God knows multiple things through a single universal concept. In this passage, Ghazali dismantles this notion by showing that distinct known objects require distinct acts of knowledge, and that Ibn Sina's attempt to have it both ways leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp Ghazali's argument flow. Notice his style: he starts by labeling the claim unintelligible.
The translation helps clarify the technical term 'secondary intention'.
2
Identify Ghazali's logical move
Find where Ghazali argues that distinct known objects necessitate distinct knowledge. He uses the mental separation argument: if knowledge of one were identical to knowledge of the other, one could not conceive of one without the other.
This is a key a priori argument against the philosophers.
3
Analyze the critique of Ibn Sina
Ghazali points out that Ibn Sina tried to avoid the problems of other philosophers but ended up in a worse contradiction. Notice how Ghazali uses shame (haya) to highlight the absurdity.
This shows Ghazali's rhetorical skill in addition to logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثانى هو أن قولكم : إن الكل معلوم له بالقصد الثانى ، كلام غير معقول ، فإنه مهما كان علمه محيطاً بغيره ، كما يحيط بذاته ، كان له معلومان متغايران ، وكان له علم بهما ، وتعدد المعلوم وتغايره ، يوجب تعدد العلم ، إذ يقبل أحد المعلومين الفصل عن الآخر فى الوهم ، فلايكون العلم بأحدهما عين العلم بالآخر ، إذ لو كان العلم بأحدهما عين العلم بالآخر ، لتعذر تقدير وجود أحدهما دون الآخر ، وليس ثم آخر ، مهما كان الكل واحداً ، فهذا لا يختلف بأن يعبر عنه بالقصد الثاني ثم ليت شعرى ، كيف يقدم على نفى الكثرة من يقول : إنه لا يعزب عن علمه مثقال ذرة فى السموات ولا فى الأرض ، إلا أنه يعرف الكل بنوع كلى ؛ والكليات المعلومة له لا تتناهى ، فيكون العلم المتعلق بها – مع كثرتها وتغايرها - واحداً من كل وجه وقد خالف « ابن سينا » فى هذا غيره من الفلاسفة ، الذين ذهبوا إلى أنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، احترازاً عن لزوم الكثرة فكيف شاركهم فى نفى الكثرة ثم باينهم في إثبات العلم بالغير ، ولما استحيا أن يقول : إن الله تعالى لا يعلم شيئاً أصلا ، لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة ، وإنما يعلم نفسه فقط ، وأما غيره فيعرفه ويعرف أيضاً نفسه وغيره ، فيكون غيره أشرف منه في العلم ؛ ترك هذا حياء من هذا المذهب ، واستنكافاً منه ، ثم لم يستحى من الإصرار على نفى الكثرة من كل وجه، وزعم أن علمه بنفسه وبغيره ، بل وبجميع الأشياء، هو مزيد ، وهو عين التناقض الذي استحيا منه سائر الفلاسفة لظهور التناقض فيه ، في أول النظر ، فإذن ليس ينفك فريق منهم عن خزى فى مذهبه وهكذا يفعل الله عز وجل بمن ضل عن سبيله ، وظن أن الأمور الإلهية يستولى على كنهها بنظره وتخيله .
The second aspect is that your statement that the whole is known to Him by secondary intention (al-qasd al-thaani) is unintelligible speech. For whenever His knowledge encompasses something other than Himself, just as it encompasses His essence, then He has two distinct known objects, and He has knowledge of them. The multiplicity and distinctness of the known objects necessitates multiplicity of knowledge, since one of the two known objects can be mentally separated from the other, so the knowledge of one is not identical to the knowledge of the other. For if the knowledge of one were identical to the knowledge of the other, it would be impossible to conceive of the existence of one without the other, while there is no other [i.e., no distinction] if the whole is one. This does not differ by expressing it as 'secondary intention'. Then I wonder how one can deny multiplicity while saying that not the weight of an atom escapes His knowledge in the heavens or the earth, but that He knows the whole by a universal mode; and the universals known to Him are endless, so knowledge related to them—despite their multiplicity and distinctness—is one in every respect. Ibn Sina differed in this from other philosophers, who held that He knows only Himself, to avoid the implication of multiplicity. How did he share with them in denying multiplicity, then diverge from them in affirming knowledge of others? And since he was too ashamed to say that God does not know anything at all, neither in this world nor the Hereafter, but knows only Himself, while others know Him and also know themselves and others, so that others would be superior to Him in knowledge; he left that out of shame for that position and disdain for it. Then he was not ashamed to insist on denying multiplicity in every respect, and claimed that His knowledge of Himself and of others, indeed of all things, is single, which is the very contradiction that other philosophers were ashamed of due to its apparent contradiction at first glance. So no group of them is free from disgrace in their position. And thus does God do with those who stray from His path and think that divine matters can be grasped by their reason and imagination.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second argument against the philosophers' claim that God knows all things through a secondary intention (al-qasd al-thaani). He argues that if God's knowledge encompasses both Himself and other things, then there are two distinct known objects, which necessitates two distinct acts of knowledge. If the knowledge of one were identical to the knowledge of the other, then one could not conceive of one without the other, but in fact we can conceive of God's essence without conceiving of other things. He criticizes the philosophers for claiming that God knows infinite universals yet His knowledge remains one in every respect. He then specifically criticizes Ibn Sina, who differed from other philosophers who said God knows only Himself. Ibn Sina wanted to affirm knowledge of others but also deny multiplicity, leading to a contradiction. Ghazali points out that Ibn Sina was too ashamed to deny all knowledge to God, but then committed a greater shame by asserting a self-contradictory position.

Key Terms
القصد الثانى
معلوم
كلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concept of 'secondary intention' solves the problem of multiplicity in God's knowledge by making it a single universal concept.
Correction: Ghazali argues that even if knowledge is called 'secondary intention', distinct known objects still require distinct knowledge, as they can be mentally separated.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that knowledge by secondary intention does not avoid multiplicity?
A) Because God knows Himself and others, so there are two distinct known objects.
B) Because secondary intention means knowledge is indirect.
C) Because the philosophers themselves disagree.
D) Because knowledge of universals is not real knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the concept of 'secondary intention' fails to resolve the logical problem of multiplicity in God's knowledge, and that Ibn Sina's position is internally contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself, like Ibn Sina, trying to hold onto a comfortable middle ground in your beliefs about Allah, ignoring a clear logical contradiction? What would it cost you to fully accept the implication of Allah's absolute oneness in your daily life?
?
Ghazali says that those who stray from Allah's path end up in disgrace, thinking they can comprehend divine matters with their own intellect. Where in your life are you trusting your reasoning more than revelation? What would it mean to humble your mind before the infinite?

Objection and Response on Knowledge of Relationals
Philosophers object that knowledge of relationals is one; Ghazali responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that knowledge of relationals can be one despite multiple objects, and Ghazali's initial response that true unity precludes multiplicity.

Core Idea
Unity of knowledge does not erase multiplicity of knowns.

The philosophers propose that if God knows Himself as a principle, that knowledge is one even though it encompasses multiple related objects. They illustrate with knowing a son, which implicitly includes knowledge of the father. Ghazali responds by asserting that genuinely unitary knowledge cannot attach to distinct objects, appealing to the philosophers' own stringent standards against multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage from 'فإن قيل' to 'وهذا محال' to grasp the full force of the philosophers' argument.
Notice how they use the example of knowing a son.
2
Identify the philosophers' examples
The philosophers give three linked arguments: (1) knowledge of father and son is one, (2) knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge are one, (3) God's infinite objects of knowledge do not multiply His knowledge.
Each example is meant to show that one knowledge can cover multiple objects.
3
Examine Ghazali's response
Read from 'قلنا' onward. Ghazali argues that if knowledge is truly one in every respect, it cannot relate to two objects. He invokes the philosophers' own principle that even essence qualified by existence would be multiplicity.
The response is cut off but the core rebuttal is clear.
4
Reflect on the philosophical stakes
Consider why this debate matters: it challenges the simplicity of God's knowledge and whether multiplicity in the known implies multiplicity in the knower.
This connects directly to divine attribute theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا ثبت أنه يعرف نفسه مبدأ على سبيل الإضافة ، فالعلم بالمضافين واحد ، إذ من عرف الابن ، عرفه بمعرفة واحدة ، وفيه العلم بالأب و بالأبوة والبنوة ضمناً ، فيكثر المعلوم ويتحد العلم ، فكذلك هو يعلم ذاته مبدأ فيتحد العلم ، وإن تعدد المعلوم ، ثم إذا عقل هذا في معلوم واحد وإضافته إليه ، ولم يوجب ذلك كثرة ، فالزيادة فيما لا يوجب جنسه كثرة لا توجب كثرة . وكذلك من يعلم الشيء ، ويعلم علمه بالشيء ، فإنه يعلمه بذلك العلم وكل علم هو علم بنفسه و بمعلومه ، فيتعدد المعلوم ويتحد العلم ، ويدل عليه أيضاً أنكم ترون أن معلومات الله تعالى لا نهاية لها ، وعلمه واحداً ، ولا تصفونه بعلوم لا نهاية لأعدادها ، فإن كان تعدد العلوم يوجب تعدد العلم فليكن في ذات الله تعالى علوم لا نهاية لأعدادها ، وهذا محال قلنا : مهما كان العلم واحداً من كل وجه ، لم يتصور تعلقه بمعلومين ، بل يقتضى ذلك كثرة ما ، على ما هو وضع الفلاسفة واصطلاحهم في تقدير الكثرة ، حتى بالغوا فقالوا : لو كانت للأول ماهية موصوفة بالوجود ، لكان ذلك كثرة ، فلم يعقلوا شيئاً واحداً له حقيقة ، ثم يوصف بالوجود ، بل زعموا :
If it is said: If it is established that He knows His essence as a principle by way of relation, then knowledge of two related things (al-mudaafayn) is one. For whoever knows a son knows him with a single knowledge, and in it implicitly lies knowledge of the father, fatherhood, and sonship. Thus the known becomes multiple while knowledge remains one. Similarly, He knows His essence as a principle, so knowledge becomes one even if the known is multiple. Moreover, if this is understood in one known object and its relation to it, and it does not necessitate multiplicity, then addition in what does not necessitate multiplicity does not necessitate multiplicity. Likewise, whoever knows a thing and knows his knowledge of the thing knows it by that same knowledge, and every knowledge is knowledge of itself and its object. Thus the known becomes multiple while knowledge remains one. This is also supported by the fact that you hold that the objects of God's knowledge are endless, and His knowledge is one, and you do not describe Him with endless sciences. For if multiplicity of knowns necessitated multiplicity of knowledge, then there would be in God's essence endless sciences, which is impossible. We say: Whenever knowledge is one in every respect, it cannot be conceived to relate to two known objects; rather, it necessitates some multiplicity, according to the philosophers' own position and convention in considering multiplicity, to the extent that they said: If the First had an essence qualified by existence, that would be multiplicity. So they did not conceive of a single thing having a reality and then being described by existence. Rather, they claimed:
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the philosophers and Ghazali's response. The philosophers argue that knowledge of two related things (like father and son) is one unitary knowledge, even though the known objects are multiple. They use the example of knowing a son, which implicitly includes knowledge of the father, yet the act of knowledge is one. Similarly, they claim, God's knowledge of Himself as a principle is one knowledge despite the multiplicity of known objects. They also argue that just as we accept that God's knowledge of endless objects is one, we must accept that knowledge can be one with multiple objects. In response (قلنا), Ghazali argues that if knowledge is truly one in every respect, it cannot relate to two distinct objects. The very fact that the philosophers themselves insisted that if the First had an essence with existence that would be multiplicity shows their own standards. Therefore, their objection fails. The response is incomplete in this window.

The philosophers' claim that one knowledge can cover multiple relational objects is refuted by Ghazali's appeal to the requirement that truly unitary knowledge cannot attach to distinct objects.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Knowledge of Multiple Things Implies Multiplicity
Ghazali argues that knowledge of multiple things requires multiple acts of knowledge, using father-son example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge of multiple objects necessarily involves multiplicity, and why this challenges the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is one and simple.

Core Idea
Knowing many things cannot be a single act—multiplicity in objects implies multiplicity in knowledge.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts the philosophical view that God's knowledge of all things is one simple essence. He argues that knowledge of multiple distinct objects requires multiple distinct acts of knowledge. Using the example of knowing a relation like father and son, he shows that even relational knowledge involves three separate knowledges. This directly challenges the claim that God's knowledge is unitary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Read the first sentence: existence (wujud) is added to essence and is other than it, so it necessitates multiplicity. This is the foundation of the argument.
Notice that Ghazali starts with a broader metaphysical principle.
2
Examine the father-son example
Ghazali illustrates with knowledge of a relation: father and son. He identifies three distinct knowledges: the essence of the son, the essence of the father, and the relation itself.
Even though the relation depends on the relata, it is a separate knowledge.
3
Apply the argument to the First (God)
If God knows Himself as the principle (mabda') of all things, He must know His own essence, the individuals of all genera and species, and the relation of principality. This results in multiplicity.
Thus, God's knowledge cannot be a single, simple act if it encompasses many objects.
4
Recognize the implications
This argument undermines the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is one and simple. Ghazali forces them to accept multiplicity in the divine knowledge or reject their premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الوجود مضاف إلى الحقيقة وهو غيره ، فيقتضى كثرة . فعلى هذا الوجه لا يمكن تقدير علم يتعلق بمعلومات كثيرة ، إلا يلزم فيه نوع كثرة أجل وأبلغ من اللازم فى تقدير وجود مضاف إلى ماهية . وأما العلم بالابن ، وكذا سائر المضافات ، ففيه كثرة ، إذ لابد من العلم بذات الابن ، وذات الأب ، وهما علمان ؛ وعلم ثالث وهو الإضافة . نعم هذا الثالث مضمن بالعلمين السابقين ، إذ هما من شروطه وضرورته ، وإلا فما لم يعلم المضاف أولا ، لا تعلم الإضافة ، فهى علوم متعددة ، بعضها مشروط بالبعض فكذلك إذا علم الأول ذاته مضافة إلى سائر الأجناس والأنواع ، لكونه مبدأ لها ، افتقر إلى أن يعلم ذاته ، وآحاد الأجناس ، وأن يعلم إضافة نفسه بالمبدئية إليها ، وإلا لم يعقل كون الإضافة معلومة له
Existence is added to the essence and is other than it, so it necessitates multiplicity. So in this manner it is not possible to conceive knowledge that relates to many known things without there being entailed in it a type of multiplicity greater and more extensive than that required in conceiving existence added to a quiddity. As for knowledge of the son, and similarly all relations, there is multiplicity in it, for there must be knowledge of the essence of the son and the essence of the father — these are two items of knowledge — and a third knowledge which is the relation. Yes, this third is implied in the two previous knowledges, for they are its conditions and necessaries; otherwise, if the related thing is not known first, the relation cannot be known. So they are multiple sciences, some conditional upon others. Likewise, if the First knows His own essence as related to all genera and species, since He is their principle, He would need to know His essence, the individuals of the genera, and to know the relation of His own principality to them; otherwise the relation being known to Him is not intelligible.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the philosophers' view that God's knowledge of many things can be one simple knowledge. Ghazali begins by establishing a general principle: existence (wujud) is an attribute added to essence (haqiqa) and is other than it, so it entails multiplicity. He then applies this to knowledge: if knowledge relates to many objects, it must entail multiplicity. He illustrates with the example of knowing a relation like father and son: one must know the essence of the son, the essence of the father (two knowledges), and the relation itself (a third knowledge). Although the third knowledge is implied by the first two, they are distinct. Similarly, if God (the First) knows Himself as the principle (mabda') of all things, He must know His own essence, the essences of all things, and the relation of principality. This would necessitate multiplicity in God's knowledge, which contradicts the philosophers' claim that His knowledge is one and simple. Ghazali follows the author's order: first the general principle, then the relational example, then the application to the First.

Key Terms
وجود
حقيقة
ماهية
إضافة
علم
معلوم
مبدئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing a relation can be reduced to knowing the relata alone, so that relational knowledge does not add multiplicity.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the relation is a third distinct knowledge, even though it depends on the prior knowledges of the relata. Thus, relational knowledge inherently introduces multiplicity.

Checkpoint

B) Two: knowledge of the son and the father
C) Three: knowledge of the son, the father, and the relation
D) Four: knowledge of the son, the father, the relation, and one's own knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that relational knowledge, such as knowing God as Creator, requires multiple distinct acts of knowledge, undermining the claim that God's knowledge is simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the mere act of knowing a relation like 'son of Zayd' requires three distinct cognitions, how much more must Allah's knowledge of your entire life story be infinitely rich? Does this make you feel more accountable knowing that each detail is distinct in His knowledge?
?
In your daily life, you are a 'relational being'—a child, a parent, a spouse. How does Ghazali's argument that relational knowledge introduces multiplicity challenge you to see each relationship as a unique object of Allah's knowledge? Will you live today with greater consciousness that every relation you have is specifically known by Him?

Knowing That One Knows Requires Another Knowledge
Ghazali refutes the claim that knowing something implies knowing that one knows by the same knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that knowledge of an object and knowledge of that knowledge are distinct acts, and why this does not lead to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Knowing that you know is a separate act of knowledge, and it stops where attention stops.

The philosophers argued that if you know something, you automatically know that you know it by the same single knowledge. This would preserve simplicity. Ghazali refutes this claim, arguing that knowing one's own knowledge is a separate act of knowledge. He then explains how this chain of knowledges terminates without infinite regress, using the example of knowing blackness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of Ghazali's refutational style.
Notice the structure: opposing view first, then denial, then explanation.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find where Ghazali states what the philosophers say: that knowing something implies knowing that one knows by the same knowledge.
This is the view he will refute.
3
Understand Ghazali's denial and alternative
Ghazali says 'it is not so' and explains that knowing one's knowledge requires another knowledge. Knowledge has multiple objects.
This is the core of his refutation.
4
Follow the example of blackness
Ghazali gives an example: one who knows blackness may be absorbed in the object and heedless of his own knowledge. If he pays attention to his knowledge, another knowledge is needed, but it stops when attention stops.
This illustrates how the chain terminates.
5
Reflect on the prevention of infinite regress
Ghazali explicitly denies infinite regress: the chain stops at a knowledge the knower is unaware of. This is the key point against the philosophers' objection.
Consider why this is important for theology and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : إن من علم شيئاً ، علم كونه عالماً ، بذلك العلم نفسه ، فيكون واحداً . فليس كذلك ، بل يعلم كونه عالماً بعلم آخر والعلم متعدداً المعلوم وينتهى إلى علم يغفل عنه ، ولا يعلمه ، ولا نقول : يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية ، بل ينقطع على علم متعلق بمعلومه ، وهو غافل عن وجود العلم المعلوم ، كالذى يعلم السواد ، وهو في حال علمه مستغرق النفس بمعلومه الذي هو سواد ، وغافل عن علمه بالسواد ، وليس ملتفتاً إليه ، فإن التفت إليه ، افتقر إلى علم آخر ، إلى أن ينقطع التفاته
As for their statement: 'Whoever knows something knows that he is knowing by that same knowledge, so it is one' — it is not so. Rather, he knows that he is knowing by another knowledge. Knowledge has multiple objects and terminates in a knowledge of which he is unaware and does not know it. And we do not say it goes on to infinity, but it stops at a knowledge related to its object, while he is heedless of the existence of the known knowledge; like one who knows blackness, and while knowing he is absorbed in his object which is blackness, and heedless of his knowledge of blackness, not paying attention to it. If he pays attention to it, he needs another knowledge, until his attention stops.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that knowledge of an object and knowledge of that knowledge are identical, thereby preserving simplicity. Ghazali denies this: knowing that one knows (knowing one's own knowledge) is a separate act of knowledge. He explains that knowledge has multiple objects: the object known and the knowledge itself. This chain of knowledges does not regress infinitely; it terminates in a knowledge of which the knower is unaware. He gives the example of knowing blackness: when one is fully absorbed in the perception of blackness, one is not simultaneously reflecting on one's own act of knowing it. The moment one attends to that knowledge, a new act of knowledge is required, and this attention can stop. Thus the philosophers' claim that one simple knowledge suffices for both the object and the awareness of knowing is false. Ghazali follows the order: first states the opposing view, then refutes it, then explains the alternative, then provides an illustration.

Key Terms

غفلة
سواد
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that self-awareness of knowledge is inherent in the act of knowing, so knowledge is one.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes them as separate knowledges; knowing the object and knowing that one knows are different acts.

Checkpoint

B) He needs another knowledge, and this can continue indefinitely
C) He needs another knowledge, but the process can stop when attention stops
D) He becomes unaware of blackness

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that knowing one's own knowledge is distinct from knowing the object, and this chain terminates in heedlessness, not infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'I know that Allah is One,' do you also know that you know it? Or are you simply absorbed in the words, heedless of your own act of knowing? Does this leave you in a state of humility, realizing your ignorance of your own certainty?
?
Think of a time you were utterly absorbed in studying a topic—perhaps a verse of the Qur'an or a proof of God's existence. Were you simultaneously aware of your own knowledge? Did that awareness ever cause you to doubt or question your grasp? If you never attended to that second knowledge, does that mean you were heedless? And if you are heedless of your own faith, what does that say about the depth of your conviction?
?
Ghazali says the chain of knowledges stops when you are heedless. Is your relationship with Allah characterized by heedless absorption in the world, or by an active awareness of your own knowledge of Him? What one concrete change could you make today to stop being absorbed in the objects of your life and instead attend to the knowledge you have of your Creator—even if that attention requires yet another step? Ghazali's Refutation of Divine Self-Knowledge Only
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that the First knows only itself as disgraceful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the philosophical view that God knows only Himself to be disgraceful and how he refutes it using comparisons to created beings.

Core Idea
If even animals know other things, a God who knows only Himself would be deficient.

In this passage, Ghazali takes on a specific philosophical position held by some 'verified' philosophers: that the First (God) knows only Himself, not His creation. He immediately calls this doctrine disgraceful and proceeds to demonstrate why. By comparing God to angels, humans, and even beasts, he shows that the view leads to an absurd conclusion: that God is inferior to His own effects. This lesson unpacks his argument and its rhetorical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the objection and Ghazali's sharp response.
The Arabic ends abruptly; focus on the complete refutation.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that Ghazali distinguishes between Ibn Sina's view (God knows other things) and the 'verified' philosophers' view (God knows only Himself). He attacks the latter.
3
Analyze Ghazali's comparative argument
Examine how Ghazali uses comparisons to angels, humans, and even beasts to show that the view implies deficiency. Then consider his rhetorical question about beauty.
This is the core of his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الإشكال إنما يلزم « ابن سينا » حيث زعم أن الأول يعلم غيره ، فأما المحققون من الفلاسفة ، فقد اتفقوا على أنه لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، فيندفع هذا الإشكال فنقول : ناهيكم خزياً بهذا المذهب ، ولولا أنه في غاية الركاكة ، لما استنكف المتأخرون عن نصرته ؛ ونحن ننبه على وجه الخزى فيه ، فإن فيه تفضيل معلولاته عليه ، إذ الملك والإنسان ، وكل واحد من العقلاء ، يعرف نفسه ومبدأه ، ويعرف غيره ؛ والأول لا يعرف إلا نفسه ، فهو ناقص بالإضافة إلى آحاد الناس ، فضلاً عن الملائكة ، بل البهيمة مع شعورها بنفسها ، تعرف أموراً أخر سواها ، ولا شك فى أن العلم شرف ، وأن عدمه نقصان ، فأين قولهم إنه عاشق ومعشوق ، لأن له البهاء الأكمل ، والجمال الأتم ، وأى جمال لوجود بسيط ، لا ما هية له ولا حقيقة ، ولا خبر له بما يجرى فى العالم ، ولا بما يلزم
If it is said: 'This problem only applies to Ibn Sina, who claimed that the First knows other than Himself. As for the verified among the philosophers, they agreed that He knows only Himself, so this problem is repelled.' We say: Sufficient for you is disgrace in this doctrine. Had it not been extremely flimsy, the later ones would not have been ashamed to support it. We shall point out the aspect of disgrace in it. For it entails preferring His effects over Him, since the angel, the human, and every rational being knows himself and his principle and knows other than himself; while the First knows only Himself. So He is deficient in comparison to individual people, let alone angels. Rather, even the beast, with its self-awareness, knows things other than itself. And there is no doubt that knowledge is an honor and its absence is a deficiency. So where is their claim that He is lover and beloved because He has perfect splendor and complete beauty? And what beauty is there for a simple existence that has no quiddity and no reality, and has no news of what happens in the world, nor of what follows?
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a fierce refutation of the view held by some philosophers (the 'verified' ones, in contrast to Ibn Sina) that God knows only Himself, not other things. Ghazali calls this doctrine disgraceful (khizyun). He first states the objection: the problem of multiplicity does not apply if God knows only Himself. He then replies sarcastically, saying this position is so weak that later philosophers were ashamed to embrace it. He identifies the disgrace: it makes God inferior to His effects. Angels, humans, and even animals know themselves and other things, whereas according to this view God knows only Himself, implying deficiency. Knowledge is an honor; its absence is a lack. Ghazali then attacks their description of God as 'lover and beloved' with perfect beauty, asking: what beauty is there in a simple existence with no quiddity, no reality, and no awareness of the world? The argument follows the structure: objection, Ghazali's response stating disgrace, then supporting reasoning with comparisons to humans and beasts, and finally a rhetorical question challenging their notion of divine beauty.

وجود بسيط

Correction: Ghazali is not demanding that God know every trivial detail; he is refuting the claim that God knows only Himself, which would imply He knows less than animals.

Ghazali's Dilemma on Divine Self-Knowledge
Ghazali argues against identifying self-knowledge with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers cannot escape multiplicity by claiming God's self-knowledge is identical to His essence, and why the analogy with human self-awareness undermines that claim.

Core Idea
Claiming God's self-knowledge is identical to His essence leads to an absurd analogy with human self-awareness.

Ghazali here tackles a key philosophical claim: that God's knowledge of Himself is exactly the same as His essence, thereby avoiding multiplicity. He presents a dilemma and uses a sharp analogy with human self-awareness to expose the logical flaw. The passage demonstrates how careful reasoning about attributes and essence prevents unwarranted theological conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the condensed style; each sentence carries a logical step.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find where Ghazali poses the question: Is God's knowledge of His essence identical to His essence or other? See how he uses this to trap the philosophers.
The dilemma forces either multiplicity (if other) or absurdity (if identical).
3
Understand the human analogy
Examine why Ghazali says claiming identity is like saying a human's self-knowledge is identical to his essence. Note the absurdity: a human can be unaware of himself, proving awareness is distinct.
This is the core of the reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
See the philosophers' objection that human self-knowledge is absent then occurs, proving otherness. Then study Ghazali's reply: otherness is not defined by temporal occurrence or conjunction.
Ghazali refines the concept of 'otherness'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final point: the eternity of God's self-knowledge does not prove identity. The imagination can still conceive essence and awareness separately.
This closes the argument against identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهؤلاء لم تتخلصوا من الكثرة ، مع اقتحام هذه المخازى أيضاً ، فإنا نقول : علمه بذاته ، عين ذاته أو غير ذاته ؟ ! فإن قلتم : إنه غير ذاته ، فقد جاءت الكثرة ، وإن قلتم : إنه عين ذاته ، فما الفصل بينكم وبين قائل : إن علم الإنسان بذاته عين ذاته ، وهذه حماقة ، إذ يعقل وجود ذاته في حالة ، هو فيها غافل عن ذاته ، ثم تزول غفلته ، ويتنبه لذاته ، فيكون شعوره بذاته غير ذاته لا محالة . فإن قلتم : إن الإنسان قد يخلو عن العلم بذاته ، فيطرأ عليه ، فيكون غيره لا محالة . فنقول : الغيرية لا تعرف بالطريان والمقارنة ، فإن عين الشيء لا يجوز أن يطرأ على الشيء ، وغير الشيء إذا قارن الشيء ، لم يصر هو هو ، ولم يخرج عن كونه غيراً ، فبأن كان الأول لم يزل عالماً بذاته لا يدل على أن علمه بذاته عين ذاته ، ويتسع الوهم لتقدير الذات ، ثم طريان الشعور ، ولو كان الذات هو بعينه لما تصور التوهم .
Then it is said to them: You have not escaped multiplicity, while also plunging into these disgraces. For we say: His knowledge of His essence—is it identical to His essence or other than His essence? If you say it is other than His essence, then multiplicity has occurred. If you say it is identical to His essence, then what distinguishes you from one who says that a human's knowledge of his self is identical to his self? This is foolishness, because his self can be conceived to exist in a state where he is unaware of his self, then his unawareness ceases and he becomes attentive to his self, so his awareness of his self is necessarily other than his self. If you say: A human can be devoid of knowledge of his self, then it occurs to him, so it is clearly something other than him. We say: Otherness is not known by temporal occurrence and conjunction. For the very essence of a thing cannot occur to the thing, and something other than the thing, when it conjoins with the thing, does not become identical to it and does not cease to be other. So the fact that the First has always been knowing His essence does not prove that His knowledge of His essence is identical to His essence. The imagination can conceive of the essence then the occurrence of awareness; if the essence were identical to awareness, imagination would not conceive this.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument against the philosophers' attempt to avoid multiplicity. Ghazali presents a dilemma: either God's knowledge of Himself is identical to His essence or distinct. If distinct, multiplicity arises (which they wanted to avoid). If identical, then it becomes analogous to claiming that human self-knowledge is identical to the human essence—which is absurd, because a human can be in a state of unawareness and then become aware, proving that the awareness is an accident distinct from the essence. The philosophers object that human self-knowledge can be absent and then occur, so it is clearly other than the essence. Ghazali responds that otherness is not determined by temporal occurrence or conjunction; the essence cannot 'occur' to itself, and if something other conjoins with it, it does not become identical. Therefore, the fact that God's self-knowledge is eternal does not prove identity. The imagination can still distinguish the essence from awareness, showing they are not identical.

Key Terms
الكثرة (multiplicity)
علمه بذاته (His knowledge of His essence)
عين ذاته (identical to His essence)
غير ذاته (other than His essence)
الطريان (temporal occurrence)
المقارنة (conjunction)
الغيرية (otherness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal self-knowledge in God necessarily implies that His knowledge of Himself is identical to His essence.
Correction: Ghazali argues that eternalness does not establish identity; logical analysis shows that essence and awareness are conceptually distinct, as demonstrated by the human analogy and the refined notion of otherness.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is it absurd to say that God's knowledge of Himself is identical to His essence?
A) It implies multiplicity in God.
B) It is analogous to saying human self-knowledge is identical to the human essence, yet humans can be unaware of themselves.
C) It denies God's simplicity.
D) It contradicts Aristotelian logic.

Key Takeaway
Eternal self-knowledge does not prove identity with the essence; logical analysis shows they are conceptually distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's self-awareness, do you unconsciously picture it as similar to your own self-awareness? How does Ghazali's argument force you to purify that concept?
